# The Treasury's Gambit - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> Monetary leaders' unconventional toolbox is about to expand

---
[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BWBh!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbd0a7d59-b456-40da-8d5b-7747a8c378ca_1076x781.jpeg)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BWBh!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbd0a7d59-b456-40da-8d5b-7747a8c378ca_1076x781.jpeg)

The _[Great Sovereign Debt Intervention](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-great-sovereign-debt-intervention)_ is here, with leaders now eager to prevent rising instability in America’s bond market. Their solutions, however, will not only prevent a calamity but stimulate risk assets without the aid of central bankers. The era of “Treasury QE” (quantitative easing) awaits us.

After we warned late last year about [rising illiquidity](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-great-sovereign-debt-intervention) in America’s sovereign bond market, leaders have begun to respond. The unintended consequences of previous policies, regulations, and interventions can no longer be ignored. Earlier this month, authorities revealed their latest mechanism to paint over the cracks in the U.S. empire’s liquidity goliath. Officials [announced](https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/jy1460) the first Treasury buyback program in just over two decades, designed to thwart illiquidity in America’s $23 trillion sovereign debt market.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pk3u!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fee62681a-20f7-4a09-b923-cb37bbef1528_4538x4081.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pk3u!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fee62681a-20f7-4a09-b923-cb37bbef1528_4538x4081.png)

the entire U.S. Treasury market visualized

Over the last decade, the post-financial crisis regulatory establishment has forced the biggest liquidity providers in the Treasury market to retreat. The major players have pulled back from market-making, just as America embarks on its greatest debt expansion in recent history. Regulations such as Basel III and the Dodd-Frank Act have [hindered](https://open.substack.com/pub/concoda/p/the-feds-global-dilemma?r=njk1&utm_campaign=post&utm_medium=web) the major liquidity giants through balance sheet constraints, such as the Supplementary Leverage Ratio (SLR) and Liquidity Coverage Ratio (LCR). The cost of making markets in Treasuries is [ever-increasing](https://www.zerohedge.com/markets/basel-iv-rules-are-coming-and-will-make-bank-lives-even-more-difficult).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YVha!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa0c5a195-39d4-400a-9480-ef8262f6014f_2156x1000.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YVha!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa0c5a195-39d4-400a-9480-ef8262f6014f_2156x1000.png)

Source: U.S. Treasury

Meanwhile, to achieve its global and domestic aims, the U.S. empire is set to issue around $1 to $2 trillion of (net) Treasury debt each year, all while the ability of financial actors to absorb such an amount is dwindling. Monetary leaders, however, have acknowledged the endgame. The rules and restrictions they have enforced over the last decade have been designed to transfer systemic risk from the big banks to the sovereign (state) level. But to also keep the global liquidity machine churning, they will need to conjure up more monetary alchemy.

Although the details aren’t yet set in stone, the U.S. Treasury will be conducting a Treasury buyback program in 2024 and onwards. It has two objectives: to improve “cash management” — jargon for reducing the volatility of the Treasury’s bank balance ([the TGA](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/WTREGEN)) — and to boost liquidity in the secondary Treasury market.

Buybacks will also enable the Treasury to replace expensive bonds with those that cost less to service. What’s more, much like the Federal Reserve, the Treasury will become a “dealer of last resort”, acting as a buyer of illiquid bonds when no other participant is willing. Using funds from either bond sales or its bank account at the Fed, the U.S. Treasury will purchase existing bonds in the secondary market (via primary dealers such as JPMorgan and Nomura) to boost liquidity and maybe even suppress yields. Lower yields equal cheaper expenses.

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zHaN!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F03970dc4-9709-4c2f-93c9-add51f4d7b71_2396x1387.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zHaN!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F03970dc4-9709-4c2f-93c9-add51f4d7b71_2396x1387.png)

Though Treasury buybacks give them the ability to greatly influence markets, officials have stated that the announced program won’t be extensive enough to act as a bailout in times of stress. If turmoil erupts, other measures will be enacted.

Today, though, the number of tools officials can employ to quash volatility has grown more limited than ever before. The Fed can’t initiate a full-blown pivot of rate cuts and QE without reigniting animal spirits, undoing all efforts to tackle inflation. Its toolbox remains scarce. Instead, officials at both the Fed and U.S. Treasury are doing everything in their power to continue a tightening cycle without reversing course. With a pivot off the table, they have been forced to implement what _Conks_ calls the “Not-QE corridor”, the new unconventional toolbox.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!h7LK!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3396ab2b-823f-48af-b017-dfa0caddcc19_1988x1687.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!h7LK!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3396ab2b-823f-48af-b017-dfa0caddcc19_1988x1687.png)

Much like the Fed’s “Global Jaws”, which depicts the global dollar rates corridor, the “Not-QE corridor” has an upper and lower limit. When the upper boundary is reached, financial conditions have become too loose for the Fed to sit by idly. More rate hikes and QT will follow. Conversely, when the lower limit is reached, financial conditions have tightened enough to warrant Fed intervention. The U.S. central bank has no intention of pivoting until it’s reached its 2% inflation target, so it must invent “stealth QE” facilities, like the [BTFP](https://www.federalreserve.gov/financial-stability/bank-term-funding-program.htm#:~:text=The%20Bank%20Term%20Funding%20Program,needs%20of%20all%20their%20depositors.) (Bank Term Funding Program).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!tkwI!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F974a55d3-80fc-4aa8-bd8e-bbdfbc566e47_2413x1637.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!tkwI!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F974a55d3-80fc-4aa8-bd8e-bbdfbc566e47_2413x1637.png)

The Fed has been using all the easing and tightening tools available in its armory, but the U.S. Treasury has yet to wield any. At the upper limit of the Not-QE corridor, the Treasury has chosen to avoid deploying “TQT”: Tighter Quantitative Tightening. With TQT, monetary leaders attempt to tighten conditions further by reducing the Fed’s balance sheet at a faster pace while issuing more long-dated bonds to the market. The greater duration risk of longer-dated bonds causes investors to sell riskier assets to fund these purchases.

Meanwhile, at the lower limit of the Not-QE corridor, the Treasury does not possess any concrete tools to stimulate asset prices. After all, that’s the job of the Federal Reserve. At the start of 2024, however, buybacks will commence, enabling the prospect of Treasury QE.

If inflation remains higher for longer, and the Fed breaks something else not worth pivoting over, the Treasury could join forces with the U.S. central bank to help ease financial conditions. While the Fed implements more Not-QE measures, the Treasury uses buybacks to stimulate risk sentiment. Previous buyback programs have not been used by Treasury officials to specifically stimulate asset prices, yet this time could be different. If politics at the Treasury and the Fed align during a period where a pivot is undesirable, the Fed’s independence could be bypassed.

The first of two mechanisms they could use to stimulate financial assets via buybacks is simple: remove duration risk from the market by buying back longer-dated bonds and swapping them with shorter maturities. Swapping 30-year bonds for 2-year notes will be stimulative. Still, to truly make this “quantitative easing for Treasuries”, officials would have to announce that this is what they’re doing in advance, thereby mimicking the psychological effects that traditional QE (buying bonds with newly printed bank reserves) has on riskier assets. Right now, the Treasury has no plans to employ this type of easing. Proposed buybacks will be “duration neutral”. But it’s feasible if desired.

Then, there’s the second Treasury QE mechanism, which this time boosts liquidity in the financial system and potentially increases risk-seeking behavior: Treasury buybacks where the newly issued bond is funded with cash drained from the Fed’s RRP (reverse repo) facility. Unlike the first kind of Treasury QE, easing occurs without Treasury officials ever intending to loosen financial conditions. If a money market fund (MMF) withdraws cash from the RRP facility, a Treasury buyback is all that’s needed to boost “net liquidity”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Sm4L!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F15423114-1e8b-4ed6-950b-cf91278fa1d9_3602x3231.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Sm4L!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F15423114-1e8b-4ed6-950b-cf91278fa1d9_3602x3231.png)

Treasury QE visualized (click to enlarge)

Before this type of Treasury QE takes place, the MMF had originally invested in RRPs (reverse repo agreements) offered by the Fed. The result was a stable stream of overnight income for the MMF but a net draining of liquidity from the financial system. RRPs are what _Conks_ calls “reserve neutralizers”. Bank reserves and deposits are “neutralized” .i.e temporarily removed from the banking system and replaced with the most illiquid cash-like investment: an RRP issued by the Fed. Unlike other short-term investments, cash is locked in the triparty repo platform — which the Fed uses to process RRP transactions — till 3:30 pm the following evening. The price to pay for receiving free money from the Fed is “trapped” intraday liquidity and often inferior returns.

When the MMF invests in an RRP, its commercial bank destroys the MMF’s deposit and sends reserves to the Fed. Once received, the U.S. central bank transforms these reserves into an RRP liability on its balance sheet. Liquidity, in the form of bank deposits and reserves, has been “neutralized”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9O8e!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2141d6e6-3190-40e5-8517-4cf8ad284548_3730x3561.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9O8e!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2141d6e6-3190-40e5-8517-4cf8ad284548_3730x3561.png)

The Fed’s RRPs remove bank deposits and reserves temporarily

But when the money fund decides to partake in a buyback by purchasing new bills the U.S. government is offering, this process “de-neutralizes” reserves and deposits, adding once-trapped RRP cash back into the banking system. Overall liquidity has expanded once again. This type of Treasury QE, however, can only be influential if MMFs are willing to unwind their RRP positions with the Fed and take part in upcoming buybacks. If investments other than RRPs, like bills and repo, offer superior returns, only then will this be stimulative. Ultimately, in both scenarios of Treasury QE, Treasury buybacks could be used as a QE-style signaling tool to further encourage investors to withdraw from money market funds (MMFs) and invest in riskier assets, thereby forcing more liquidity into the banking system.

Upon implementing Treasury QE, monetary leaders will have fitted the missing piece in their Not-QE corridor:

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VVSv!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe7d5d8ba-9649-455b-b24d-b6052d27cc0d_1988x1687.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VVSv!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe7d5d8ba-9649-455b-b24d-b6052d27cc0d_1988x1687.png)

Treasury QE via buybacks and “Not-QE” from the Fed will provide a stronger lower bound to ease financial conditions when needed, while QT and TQT achieve the opposite effect. Moreover, if the Fed and Treasury have joint political goals, they can effectively bypass the Fed’s so-called independence, coordinating the easing and tightening of financial conditions. On the flip side, they could also offset one another’s actions. For instance, the Treasury could be “tightening” while the Fed “eases”.

For now, monetary leaders only need to activate some elements of the Not-QE corridor. U.S. Treasury officials have decided not to fire up TQT (Tighter Quantitative Tightening), while “Treasury QE” through buybacks could only come online after 2024 commences. QT (quantitative tightening) and “Not-QE”, meanwhile, remain active. QT is still shrinking the Fed’s balance sheet by $95 billion per month, whereas “Not-QE” is delivering a somewhat stimulative effect to risk assets, without the Fed ever printing reserves to buy bonds outright (actual QE).

Nobody, not even the Fed, seems to know what catalyst will cause officials to finally return to rate cuts, QE, or both. If inflation remains stickier for longer than most expect, the Not-QE corridor won’t collapse anytime soon, and monetary alchemy will prosper. The Treasury market provides those who oversee it the power to control global finance like never before. Yet they’ve chosen not to use it to its full effect. But with Treasury QE on the way, those in charge may give in to temptation. Eventually, these powers may be activated. It’s widely believed that officials are running out of ideas and tools to preserve stability. Yet in reality, the golden age of intervention is yet to come. When Treasury QE arrives, the Great Sovereign Debt Intervention™ will have only just begun.

_**If you enjoyed this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for supporting macro journalism!**_

# The Transitory Pause - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> how a combination of the Fed's silence and inaction has been influencing markets

---
The much-anticipated liquidity drain is in motion, with many headlines predicting turbulence ahead for markets. Yet, its effects may not deliver the impact that many believe. Numerous hidden forces have emerged, acting against a liquidity squeeze. The “Transitory Pause” is here.

After an epic rise in stock prices in the first half of 2023, investors are wondering if the second half will produce the opposite outcome. A liquidity-fueled rally has driven the S&P500 up 12% so far this year. But now, the next significant “liquidity drain” is about to begin. The latest debt limit drama has resulted in a debt ceiling suspension till 2025, allowing monetary leaders to fire up the printing press once again. Over the remainder of 2023, the U.S. Treasury will now issue around a mammoth ~$1 trillion in t-bills — Treasuries with a one-year expiry or less — into the most systemically important market. If history repeats, officials will aim to fill the U.S. government’s bank account, the Treasury General Account (TGA) within the Federal Reserve System, with around $600 billion by September. Holding the master key to every commercial bank’s Fed account, the TGA will slowly amass reserves.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!77cJ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fed60643c-57c6-4b7f-9712-d1a746b0a0b7_3774x2940.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!77cJ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fed60643c-57c6-4b7f-9712-d1a746b0a0b7_3774x2940.png)

The consensus is concerned over two effects of the “TGA refill”: a large issuance of government debt prompting market instability, and the subsequent draining of bank deposits and reserves reducing liquidity. The outcomes of both, however, aren’t as scary as they appear. First, it’s believed that issuing such a large quantity of t-bills in a short space of time will be difficult for markets to absorb, blowing out spreads and provoking turmoil. But as history shows, bond markets absorb huge issuances, even within a month, without much hassle.

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2pgY!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F714db12e-e636-4571-b47a-347ad35ecdb2_1176x620.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2pgY!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F714db12e-e636-4571-b47a-347ad35ecdb2_1176x620.png)

Source: JPMorgan

As for demand, with yields offering the highest return in decades, plus the switch from an unsecured (LIBOR) to a secured (SOFR) monetary standard [in full swing](https://concoda.substack.com/p/the-silent-monetary-revolution), the world is eager to chomp on America’s ever-increasing debt load. Financial behemoths are [hungrier than ever](https://twitter.com/cmegroup/status/1643352320212647942?s=46). We also know in advance that major market players are willing to consume a large batch of sovereign debt by referring to the Treasury’s latest survey of the Fed’s “primary dealers”, specific entities the central bank mandates to make markets in Treasuries. Expectations are in line with demand.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zm0A!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F57aed0c9-9df2-411c-90b8-fc5bf8aaebf1_2612x1387.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zm0A!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F57aed0c9-9df2-411c-90b8-fc5bf8aaebf1_2612x1387.png)

Instead, it’s not whether market participants will be able to absorb trillions in new issuance but who buys the majority of Treasuries issued that will influence markets. The real concern is the subsequent liquidity drain from the banking system. Will this prompt another liquidity squeeze? It’s not as clear-cut.

The most bullish scenario for liquidity is if most t-bills are bought with cash stored mostly by money market funds (MMFs) and some banks in the Fed’s RRP (reverse repo) facility. This is where, after factoring in regulation and risk vs. reward, most excess cash ends up.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!wGVw!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3d9b206b-e91b-430f-9b57-011508a6f8db_2597x1819.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!wGVw!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3d9b206b-e91b-430f-9b57-011508a6f8db_2597x1819.png)

For the uninitiated, repo (short for repurchase agreements) is a market for borrowing securities, usually Treasuries, for a fee overnight or over another brief period. An RRP (reverse repo) is simply the trade from the opposite perspective: cash is lent, secured by collateral, and earns interest.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5LOu!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Faef7f388-d224-42b9-ab69-d8a041ecc4e9_2781x2388.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5LOu!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Faef7f388-d224-42b9-ab69-d8a041ecc4e9_2781x2388.png)

RRPs conducted by the Fed are the most illiquid type of money market investment available. While bills and most other repo agreements can be liquidated intraday to raise cash, the RRP “traps” liquidity by only allowing access to it after 3:30 pm. The TGA refill could cause MMFs to unwind RRPs, transforming illiquid RRP liabilities on its balance sheet into highly liquid reserves.

Still, this won’t increase liquidity in the banking system. It will only transform one form of trapped liquidity into another. Just like when it initially invested in an RRP, the MMF’s t-bill payment removes deposits from the banking system and “neutralizes” reserves, which this time are sent to the U.S. government’s bank account at the Fed (the TGA), withdrawing liquidity from the private sector.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2j_p!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8ee11fa0-9288-4040-8e28-dd9fb9415767_3730x3525.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2j_p!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8ee11fa0-9288-4040-8e28-dd9fb9415767_3730x3525.png)

Reserves are “neutralized” since the cash can’t be deployed by private entities into the economy or financial assets. As for the t-bill the MMF bought, it’s highly unlikely to liquidate it — around 8% likely to be exact. Thus, after a “TGA refill bill” has been funded by RRP cash, “liquidity” remains neutral (or equal).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftdQ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3db2cb9c-4ac1-42a5-8fc7-aaa4b6d62e56_3596x3351.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftdQ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3db2cb9c-4ac1-42a5-8fc7-aaa4b6d62e56_3596x3351.png)

(click to enlarge)

It’s not as easy for MMFs, however, to simply pull money from the RRP to fund new t-bills. They have to be incentivized to do so. MMFs, after all, are investment vehicles looking to maximize their returns while under the watch of regulators. Specific conditions are required. For a sizeable “RRP drain” to even have a chance of occurring, 1-month bill yields must rise not only above the RRP rate but also above SOFR (the Secured Overnight Financing Rate), the rate for private repos — some of which happen to be conducted in [triparty repo](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/the-dynamics-of-the-us-overnight-triparty-repo-market-20210802.html), the same platform the Fed uses for RRP trades.

Due to regulation, government-only MMFs, the most common type of MMF, prefer investing in overnight repos (SOFR and RRP) rather than 1-month bills. These funds must maintain a WAM (weighted average maturity) of 60 days or less, and overnight repos count as a zero-day maturity. Overnight repos also provide ample day-to-day liquidity while juicing returns.

Consequently, the spread between bill yields and repo (SOFR and RRP) must widen significantly for MMFs to find bills tempting. Thus, the chance that they step up is lower than many envision.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!r9L3!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F68cb5130-dd35-4e47-a129-3d42bf2e51ee_3394x1899.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!r9L3!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F68cb5130-dd35-4e47-a129-3d42bf2e51ee_3394x1899.png)

_\*SOFR can drop the RRP rate on rare occasions .i.e large cash/collateral imbalances_

If MMFs aren’t tempted to absorb bills using RRP cash, failing to dampen the effects of a TGA refill, a sea of other willing buyers will step in, from onshore corporate treasurers to offshore banks to international organizations. If so, deposits and reserves will tumble. But the decrease in reserves and deposits caused by these entities ingesting billions in bonds is not the only type of liquidity impacting financial markets. While the narrative of a “TGA refill” crushing risk assets has risen to prominence, opposing forces have emerged.

Not only have we moved toward the upper limit of, what Conks calls, the Not-QE Corridor, signifying a loosening of financial conditions. Silently, the Fed has temporarily withdrawn from signaling tightness. With the “[Fed skip](https://abcnews.go.com/US/wireStory/skip-pause-federal-reserve-raise-rates-week-month-99893525)” in play, the Transitory Pause is now in session.

A Transitory Pause is a combination of the Fed’s inaction and silence around enforcing an adequate tightening of financial conditions. The market has been repressed by heightened volatility and uncertainty for so long, that when it sniffs out dovishness, risk assets respond. Previous measures the Fed has implemented to quell financial conditions have proven ineffective, or even defunct, and fail to discourage animal spirits. QT (quantitative tightening), which on its own has failed to achieve actual tightening, is even offset by other [forces](https://twitter.com/steve_donze/status/1667004786259140610?s=20). Meanwhile, a sharp hiking regime has been replaced by the idea that the Fed could possibly pause for good, thereby enabling volatility to tumble and flows to resume into financial assets, unabated.

With the Transitory Pause in effect, the finance world’s natural long bias has awoken once again. Retirement funds, no longer factoring in heightened volatility and rate hike intensity, have stopped rebalancing out of stocks into bonds, while entities that load up on stocks solely on falling volatility have been given a green light. As for dollar funding markets, bond vol plummeting from its highs has likely [spurred](https://concoda.substack.com/p/money-market-brief) more activity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8OHC!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F57610aaa-296b-408e-ba32-b1dba25542ed_3696x2440.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8OHC!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F57610aaa-296b-408e-ba32-b1dba25542ed_3696x2440.png)

The icing on the cake for the risk asset (or, at least, mega-cap tech/AI) rally was the Federal Reserve’s recent stillness. What’s more, the market has likely been frontrunning the “[blackout period](https://www.stlouisfed.org/en/about-us/resources/blackout-periods#:~:text=Federal%20Reserve%20policy%20limits%20the,a%20meeting%20unless%20otherwise%20noted.),” which currently limits FOMC participants and staff’s ability to speak publicly or be questioned. The “Transitory Pause” has now approached maximum potency.

If the “Not-QE” era persists, front-running the Fed’s silence and inaction to tighten will become a regular window in which risk assets soar. The “Transitory Pause” will eventually become a defining theme in markets. But right now, we’re only within an early iteration. The Fed will once again intervene and reiterate its tightening stance, which they’ve seemingly forsaken over the past few months. The latest Transitory Pause will likely end, not via the effects of a “TGA refill”, but by a more powerful trigger: the return of a hawkish Fed.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for supporting macro journalism!**_

# Explainer: Demystifying Money Markets - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a primer on the rates powering the monetary plumbing

---
We thought it would be valuable to release a guide for our readers to help them navigate our upcoming and future articles without being in the dark on some essential money market terminology. In the first of many “subscriber explainers”, we’ll provide an overview, in plain English, of every major rate in money markets, while also debunking the jargon you will unfortunately encounter.

To help accomplish this, below is an infographic from our upcoming piece, an overview of the most crucial dollar funding market: repo. We’ll use it to demystify the various rates that make up the arcane U.S. money market complex.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2Z--!,w_2400,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5dc2560c-f7ef-4526-96da-7b80e3307bc3_7386x8120.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2Z--!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5dc2560c-f7ef-4526-96da-7b80e3307bc3_7386x8120.png)

But before we begin, some context. Money markets are markets for lending and borrowing money efficiently. For this to occur, dealers must provide liquidity by running “matched books,” which look like a typical balance sheet (_see the “triparty repo market makers” section in the infographic_). Dealers borrow cash using a repo and lend it out in a reverse repo, earning a profit from the spread they charge — the difference between the rate they lent and borrowed.

Going forward, everything makes a lot more sense when you think in terms of liquidity providers making markets (and trying to earn a spread) by intermediating between cash borrowers and cash lenders.

Now, with that in mind, let’s decipher every money market rate.

_need a recap of what a repo is? [click here](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdf6f675f-fe9c-482e-85ae-1783a892bbc5_2781x2388.png)_

First, we have the private complex in repo. These market rates (Triparty, GCF, and DVP) are determined by private repo participants but are still influenced, to a large degree, by the Federal Reserve’s monetary policies and “alphabetti spaghetti”-named facilities — like the Fed’s [RRP](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/rrp_faq).

“Private rates” is also a bit misleading, as repo markets are fairly regulated and most transactions are now backed by government securities, primarily U.S. Treasuries and debt securities issued by government-sponsored enterprises, such as Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac, and Ginnie Mae.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!rdsp!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc9ed80c2-9adc-4f59-848a-24861ef2e9bd_900x575.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!rdsp!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc9ed80c2-9adc-4f59-848a-24861ef2e9bd_900x575.png)

The aim of Triparty repo (which simply means three entities involved) is for cash lenders to lend cash to cash borrowers without worrying about what specific security backs the repo loan, a so-called “general collateral” (GC) repo. Triparty GC repo transactions are negotiated between two parties but are settled via a clearing bank. Trades are processed through the [Bank of New York Mellon](https://www.bnymellon.com/emea/en.html) (BNYM), the sole triparty agent in the repo market. JPMorgan used to run a triparty service but, because of regulatory constraints, [pulled out](https://www.pionline.com/article/20160816/ONLINE/160819924/j-p-morgan-exit-from-repo-market-seen-as-further-regulatory-fallout) in 2016.

BNYM takes custody of the cash and securities during Triparty trades, values and monitors collateral, reports trade status to both parties, and settles balances and assets once finished.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PkM6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4ad0fd9-e4bf-4a95-a992-b3c782e628e1_977x698.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PkM6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4ad0fd9-e4bf-4a95-a992-b3c782e628e1_977x698.png)

Source: BNYM

Typically, the TPR rate is the cash borrowing rate for primary dealers and the cash lending rate for asset managers like money market funds (MMFs). MMFs will lend cash to primary dealers — who will use the cash to pay for the Treasury backing the repo loan. The primary dealer will then lend cash to other dealers in other repo segments, forming a “matched book”. In the main infographic, you can see these flows in green, blue, and red. Primary dealers will charge a higher spread to lend to different participants. Hedge funds, holding little market power, pay the most, and other large dealers pay the least.

Many types of entities transact in triparty repo…

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dCQf!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89e6ff12-0a64-4d5c-ab44-58105d84d3f4_630x376.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dCQf!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89e6ff12-0a64-4d5c-ab44-58105d84d3f4_630x376.png)

…but none, apart from maybe commercial banks and securities lenders, make up a sizeable part of the market compared to primary dealers, MMFs, and, of course, the Federal Reserve. Triparty repo is the venue for the Fed’s infamous RRP (reverse repo) facility, which explains the large increase in Triparty volume after March 2021. Regulation (the SLR) pushed money out of the banking system and into MMFs that invest in RRPs offered by the Fed. You can read more about the mechanics behind this move [here](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-hidden-put).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nhSP!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F98c589a2-1d58-497b-83bd-ff3db58d362e_900x575.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nhSP!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F98c589a2-1d58-497b-83bd-ff3db58d362e_900x575.png)

What’s more, although it seems like it, Triparty is _not_ the largest repo market. We’ll get to that one later.

First, what does “General Collateral Financing**”** mean?

In General Collateral Financing (GCF) repo, dealers lend cash to other dealers, who pledge (almost) any type of security as collateral to secure the trade — “secure the trade” meaning that in the event of default, the lender receives the borrower’s collateral. The cash lender simply wants to lend cash and doesn’t care (within market rules) about the type of security they receive that secures the loan. Usually, this means the cash lender will receive a random Treasury bill/note with a random maturity as collateral.

GCF is a centrally-cleared, interdealer market run by the Fixed Income Clearing Corporation (FICC), which uses the BNYM as a triparty custodian. The GCF rate is the “volume-weighted average interest rate” of all repos starting on a given day in the FICC’s GCF Service. In English, this reflects the cost of borrowing cash raised by dealers from institutional investors that is lent to other dealers.

GCF is dominated by the Fed’s primary dealers and domestic U.S. banks, who borrow money from cash lenders in triparty at the TPR (triparty rate) and lend it to other primary dealers, non-primary dealers, and foreign banks at the GCF rate, thereby earning a spread. In the main infographic, you can see the flows in blue and green. _(It is, of course, possible to fund GCF trades with cash from markets other than the popular choice of triparty repo.)_

Transactions from this market are used to calculate some of the Fed’s reference rates (more on this below).

DVP (Delivery Versus Payment) is a quirky way to describe such a simple concept as exchanging cash for specific securities. DVP is simply the centrally-cleared “Bilateral” Repo Market run by the FICC.

“Bilateral” repo is a dealer-to-customer repo market, where the specific security pledged as collateral is desirable to the cash lender. Just like other segments, dealers connect cash lenders and cash borrowers. Hedge funds, however, are the major borrowers and lenders in DVP, because of this segment’s ability to facilitate leveraged trades. They know the exact collateral they are promised from cash borrowers, which is usually the [cheapest to deliver](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/cheapesttodeliver.asp) (CTD) into a futures contract. Sourcing a CTD security is the backbone of the most popular leveraged trade among relative value hedge funds, known as the “[Treasury-Futures Basis](https://www.financialresearch.gov/working-papers/files/OFRwp-21-01-hedge-funds-and-the-treasury-cash-futures-disconnect.pdf)”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VkWR!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa33c8b8c-64a0-4410-bf89-3ecfe6c4d004_7072x4269.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VkWR!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa33c8b8c-64a0-4410-bf89-3ecfe6c4d004_7072x4269.png)

The dispersion of rates in DVP is the widest. Hedge funds will not only be charged the highest spread from primary dealers, who’ve borrowed from cash lenders in triparty (see the red flows in the infographic). They will be borrowing at incredibly lower rates in the “specials” market (see the specials section in the infographic). This is where the trading of securities subject to high demand in the repo and cash markets occurs. Rates on specials fall below even the ON RRP rate set by the Fed (the so-called risk-free rate in repo).

Certain entities, custodian banks with $5 billion or more in equity capital and other large dealers, sponsor certain clients, mostly hedge funds and asset managers, to trade on the FICC’s centrally-cleared platform. The major benefit is that entities can trade more repos without increasing the size of their balance sheet (also known as [netting](https://www.eurex.com/resource/blob/2602520/8747aa5743eea41f02aa1871025e3ede/data/balance-sheet-netting.pdf)), hence not incurring balance sheet costs (think Basel III).

[

![BɅRTON on Twitter: "Is it QE or not QE? 1/10 Today, Powell said the  following https://t.co/Ej4Np7Pj6O https://t.co/LP1HSCKkln" / Twitter](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gsJZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5f054851-8d13-4412-9a5f-bf260ee03407_2448x1383.jpeg "BɅRTON on Twitter: "Is it QE or not QE? 1/10 Today, Powell said the  following https://t.co/Ej4Np7Pj6O https://t.co/LP1HSCKkln" / Twitter")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gsJZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5f054851-8d13-4412-9a5f-bf260ee03407_2448x1383.jpeg)

As of writing, the FICC runs a Sponsored service in the GCF repo service and the DVP (cleared bilateral) repo service.

One rate is missing from the official statistics: the NCCBR (non-centrally cleared bilateral repo) rate. Presently, not enough data has been collected to produce an _official_ dataset on this segment, which is odd, as it’s the biggest repo venue by far!

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qemw!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2d8f5b70-2524-4894-b249-efa81c844551_1501x1239.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qemw!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2d8f5b70-2524-4894-b249-efa81c844551_1501x1239.png)

Just like cleared bilateral repo (the FICC’s DVP service), dealers connect cash lenders and cash borrowers, with hedge funds being the major borrowers and lenders in NCCBR — again, because of this segment’s ability to facilitate leveraged trades.

NCCBR also includes repo activity from some broker-dealers, banks, REITs, pension funds, sovereign wealth funds, and insurance companies.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oRFW!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fef2723f5-2e48-4632-a2fa-23df4bc0ea6a_1476x1442.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oRFW!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fef2723f5-2e48-4632-a2fa-23df4bc0ea6a_1476x1442.png)

Next, we have the Fed’s reference rates, which attempt to accurately measure money market funding costs. If you’ve read our money market updates, you’ll know about the “U.S. money market rates complex” chart we publish. This includes all the rates we’ll now explain.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dSuB!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fba6eca9b-6bd9-4ddd-bce4-28b64a6b6f0a_6157x4578.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dSuB!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fba6eca9b-6bd9-4ddd-bce4-28b64a6b6f0a_6157x4578.png)

This is the risk-free rate for financial institutions that get paid interest on reserves they park at the Fed. Banks get paid IORB **(**Interest On Reserve Balances), but GSEs (government-sponsored enterprises) that have reserve accounts, like Federal Home Loan Banks, do not. The Fed pays interest on reserve balances at an annualized rate (5.15% at the time of writing), higher than even the unsecured Fed Funds rate (EFFR).

IORB is the successor of IOER (Interest On Excess Reserves) and IORR (Interest On Required Reserves). In the Fed’s “ample reserve regime” (.i.e a large balance sheet and a large number of reserves), there are no “excess” reserves _(excess reserves here meaning the number of reserves on hand that exceed the amount needed to satisfy the Fed’s reserve requirements)_ because reserve requirements have been set to 0%. Subsequently, both IOER/IORR have grown redundant, prompting the Fed to replace them with IORB.

Reserve requirements were abolished simply because banks have so many reserves and now sometimes need to hold large reserve balances to comply with regulations, such as the [LCR](https://www.bis.org/fsi/fsisummaries/lcr.htm).

The least significant, most uneventful market in dollar funding happens to be the Fed’s key policy rate: EFFR, the Effective Federal Funds Rate. After years of the regulatory establishment’s increased scrutiny of interbank lending, banks have almost fled the Fed Funds market. If you look closely, you’ll see the thing light green/dark brown lines showing the amount of Fed Funds purchased (borrowed) and sold (lent) relative to other assets and liabilities on banks’ balance sheets.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!W59w!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9975802d-d2b7-4e24-a386-51280cad4706_1402x963.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!W59w!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9975802d-d2b7-4e24-a386-51280cad4706_1402x963.png)

Source; Federal Reserve

The only entities that still operate in this market are those engaging in “IORB arbitrage”. Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs) with access to a Fed master account — but don’t get paid interest on holding reserves — lend Fed Funds to foreign banks who deposit these reserves at the Fed overnight to earn a spread (IORB-EFFR is currently yielding 0.10%). It’s only foreign banks because they are not subject to the same stringent regulations as U.S. banks and do not pay FDIC insurance fees. Thus, they can realize a profit from the arbitrage, unlike others.

Today, secured lending (repo) has become the norm, and unsecured lending (LIBOR/EFFR) has been almost forbidden.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Gsr3!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffb1902c2-7ad3-4f7b-8b56-76a5eefde731_5352x3805.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Gsr3!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffb1902c2-7ad3-4f7b-8b56-76a5eefde731_5352x3805.png)

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!SZ02!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F23b1f7b9-6ffb-4f73-a65e-125a215fa294_2576x1672.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!SZ02!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F23b1f7b9-6ffb-4f73-a65e-125a215fa294_2576x1672.png)

LIBOR and Fed Funds are now insignificant. Superseding their relevancy is SOFR (the Secured Overnight Financing Rate) — the rate to borrow cash (to fund dollar-based activities) secured against U.S. government collateral overnight — which we’ll get to shortly.

The overnight bank funding rate is an underwhelming name for what really is a global dollar rate. While EFFR is overnight interbank lending between entities with a Federal Reserve master account, OBFR is lending between banks and offshore banks. It’s essentially a measure of wholesale, unsecured, overnight bank funding costs, calculated using federal funds transactions, certain Eurodollar transactions, and certain domestic deposit transactions — reported in the [FR 2420 Report of Selected Money Market Rates](https://www.federalreserve.gov/apps/reportingforms/Report/Index/FR_2420).

While the Fed Funds market is, as the Fed states, “domestic unsecured borrowing in dollars by depository institutions from other depository institutions and certain other entities, primarily government-sponsored enterprises”, the Eurodollar market is “unsecured dollar deposits held at banks or bank branches outside of the United States”. Foreign banks with New York branches have sizeable interoffice positions, functioning as a meaningful link between onshore funding markets and offshore Eurodollars. Because of this, EFFR and OBFR trade side by side most of the time.

Fun fact: OBFR was once a [potential candidate](https://www.sec.gov/spotlight/fixed-income-advisory-committee/arrc-faqs-041519.pdf) for a LIBOR replacement, but regulators decided on SOFR (the Secured Overnight Financing Rate) instead.

The Tri-Party General Collateral Rate (TGCR) is a measure of rates on “overnight, specific-counterparty tri-party general collateral repurchase agreement (repo) transactions secured by Treasury securities”. In plain English, it is repo transactions conducted on the BNYM Triparty platform, where the specific securities provided as collateral are not identified until other terms are agreed upon. Cash lenders don’t care about the security pledged as long as it’s high-quality government paper.

This rate measures transactions in the “Triparty Repo” section of the infographic and is the primary borrowing rate for primary dealers and large dealers while being the primary lending rate for asset managers looking to invest their cold, hard dollars.

The BGCR includes all trades in the TGCR (Tri-Party General Collateral Rate), but also GCF Repo transactions. This illustrates the median rate being transacted today in the “Triparty Repo” and “GCF Repo” sections of the infographic. Only 8% of repo volumes occur in GCF repo, thus TGCR and BGCR tend to equal each other and overlap (as seen in the U.S. money markets complex).

SOFR (Secured Overnight Financing Rate) is the broadest measure of repo rates available. It includes all the data from TGCR and BGCR (Triparty repo transactions at the BNYM plus the FICC’s GCF repo service) but also trades from the FICC’s DVP service, or cleared bilateral repo.

In cleared bilateral repo, it’s mainly dealers sourcing specific securities for the “buy-side” .i.e hedge funds. Because of this, the dispersion of rates is greater in SOFR — rates print much higher and much lower than in other markets. Hence, SOFR tends to print a couple of basis points higher than TGCR/BGCR. What’s more, the Fed’s SOFR methodology cuts out “specials” trades, which due to borrowers’ desire to obtain specific securities, repo rates trade much lower, even below the Fed’s ON RRP (the risk-free rate in repo).

The ON RRP is the overnight rate the Fed pays on reserve repos or RRPs. In an RRP, the Desk (the name for the Fed’s trading desk) sells a security to eligible counterparties — primary dealers, banks, money market funds, and government-sponsored enterprises — and repurchases that same security the next day. Entities simply contact the Fed between 12:45 pm to 1:15 pm ET (or in an emergency, basically whenever they want) and submit an amount of up to $160 billion that they want to lend to the Fed overnight. As with private triparty repos, the Fed uses the triparty platform at the BNYM for RRP trades, so cash is settled at 3:30 pm the next day.

Since the Fed is effectively adding a floor to repo rates with the ON RRP, this is the risk-free rate of the repo market. The Fed has also mandated that its reverse repos are risk-free alternative investments, which presumably means a substitute for Treasury bills or private repos whenever they are in scarce supply. Repo rates therefore (in theory) shouldn’t fall below ON RRP unless the financial system is flushed with excess cash, which coincidentally is what is happening at the time of writing! With the Fed’s TGCR and BGCR now printing below ON RRP, we know 2023 is an “excess cash” era…

That’s a wrap! All the major money market rates have been covered. If you have any queries or need clarification on any minor detail, leave a comment below.

_**Thanks for reading.**_ _**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for supporting macro journalism!**_

# The Death of the Interbank Market - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a primer on the demise of Eurodollar and Fed Funds lending

---
After multiple significant crises, from a global pandemic to a subprime mortgage meltdown, the inner workings of the monetary system have changed forever. Monetary leaders have vowed to protect systemically important financial giants from collapse, implementing what _Conks_ calls The Big Three™: [The Dodd-Frank Act](https://www.cftc.gov/LawRegulation/DoddFrankAct/index.htm) (removing banks’ excessive risk-taking abilities), [The Basel Framework](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/basell-iii.asp#:~:text=Basel%20III%20is%20an%20international,being%20implemented%20as%20of%202022.) (discouraging systemic risk and high levels of leverage), and the SEC’s [money market reform](https://investor.vanguard.com/investor-resources-education/mutual-funds/money-market-reform#:~:text=Money%20market%20reform%20is%20a,retail%2C%20government%2C%20and%20institutional.) (substituting riskier dollar funding sources with securer alternatives).

This assembly of rules and regulations has prompted Wall Street giants to withdraw from operating as major intermediaries and excessive speculators. Their role as risk-takers has been reassigned to the shadow banking layer, with securities dealers and other “shadow entities”, jargon for [non-banks](https://www.worldbank.org/en/publication/gfdr/gfdr-2016/background/nonbank-financial-institution), absorbing banks’ now-prohibited operations.

As a result, a unique monetary paradigm has emerged. Banks no longer want to lend to each other, and even so, regulators won’t let them. Meanwhile, free from similar regulatory constraints, the [shadow banking layer](https://www.centralbank.ie/consumer-hub/explainers/what-is-shadow-banking#:~:text=Shadow%20banking%20is%20a%20term,function%20to%20traditional%20bank%20lending.) has proliferated, rising to over [half](https://www.barrons.com/articles/shadow-banks-account-for-half-of-the-worlds-assetsand-pose-growing-risks-8f4b5961) the world’s assets. Secured dollar funding markets (like [repo](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/repurchaseagreement.asp)) have expanded, while unsecured markets (such as [commercial paper](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/commercialpaper.asp#:~:text=Commercial%20paper%20is%20a%20form,average%20of%20around%2030%20days.)) have halved in volume. The number of monetary transformations has kept ascending and shows no signs of stopping.

Perhaps the most unforeseen shift, however, has been the gradual demise of what was once the most crucial dollar funding market: the market for [Federal Funds](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/f/federalfunds.asp#:~:text=Key%20Takeaways,and%20others%20with%20too%20little.).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!q--e!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb2ce16b2-06af-429e-9a43-d1faab8f402c_7556x4515.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!q--e!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb2ce16b2-06af-429e-9a43-d1faab8f402c_7556x4515.png)

_major flows in the Fed Funds market (as of today)_

The Federal Funds (Fed Funds) market enables trading of Federal Reserve “[settlement balances](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/account_settlement.asp#:~:text=An%20account%20settlement%20generally%20refers,in%20any%20of%20the%20accounts.),” money created exclusively for entities under the U.S. government’s umbrella, primarily commercial banks but also government-sponsored enterprises (like Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac), [thrifts](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/t/thrift.asp), U.S. branches of foreign banks, and [foreign official institutions](https://www.nasdaq.com/glossary/f/foreign-official-institutions#:~:text=Central%20governments%20of%20foreign%20countries,stabilization%20fund%3B%20diplomatic%20and%20consular). When U.S. and foreign citizens pay for goods and services in U.S. dollars, they use bank deposits created by commercial banks. Behind the scenes, though, the Federal Reserve System debits and credits the associated “[Fed Master Accounts](https://www.frbservices.org/financial-services/accounting/service-setup/master-account.html)” — bank accounts issued to every entity with access to the Federal Reserve System — of every party involved in a transaction.

The rationale is to guarantee payment settlement among entities systemic to the global dollar banking system in _all_ environments, but especially during financial crises. After decades of America’s private sector acting as an inadequate lender (and dealer) of last resort, the state has taken over the role of interbank (or “inter-Fed system”) payments. Subsequently, the banking system has become multi-layered: banks settle customer payments, and the central bank settles interbank payments.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!R8jf!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd3654649-0d36-4739-87f4-aa069b0927c8_2109x1615.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!R8jf!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd3654649-0d36-4739-87f4-aa069b0927c8_2109x1615.png)

Armed with state backing, Fed Funds — also known as “reserves”, “reserve balances”, “[Deposits with F.R. Banks](https://www.federalreserve.gov/releases/h41/current/h41.htm)”, and the aforementioned “settlement balances” — are the most liquid form of U.S. dollars available. Even if an entity runs out of reserves to settle a payment, its local Federal Reserve bank will lend Fed Funds against high-quality collateral (usually U.S. Treasuries or [agency securities](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/agencybonds.asp)). What’s more, reserves can also be converted into cold, hard physical banknotes, known officially as “vault cash”, by commercial banks to process customer [ATM](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/atm.asp#:~:text=ATM%2C%20or%20automated%20teller%20machine,account%20without%20visiting%20a%20teller.) withdrawals. The Fed and its affiliates send an armored truck full of U.S. dollars to the bank’s requested branch, debiting its Fed Master Account as payment.

Trading of Fed Funds commenced in the 1920s, with only a few Federal Reserve member banks in New York City participating. A century of financial chaos and alchemy later, however, and the number of Fed Funds account holders had skyrocketed. Thousands of domestic and foreign financial institutions were joined by government sponsored-enterprises (GSEs) — Freddie Mac, Fannie Mae, Ginnie Mae, and the majority of Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs), plus many savings and loan associations.

Up until the subprime meltdown, the Fed Funds market was flourishing. Volumes regularly [exceeded](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2013/12/6a01348793456c970c019b01bdca9a970d.jpg) $200 billion daily. On a typical day, often falling short of reserves, the Wall Street megabanks topped up their master accounts by borrowing excess Fed Funds from their smaller (usually regional bank) counterparts. But then the financial crisis changed everything, marking the beginning of the end of a bustling Fed Funds market. With the Big Three™ looming, interbank lending was doomed. The Fed Funds market — and along with it, the Eurodollar market — was about to transform.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!fxc8!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2239848d-5d59-4ada-984f-ee84906cede3_505x376.jpeg)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!fxc8!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2239848d-5d59-4ada-984f-ee84906cede3_505x376.jpeg)

_Source: New York Fed_

Unlike the years and quarters that followed, the immediate aftermath of the collapse of Lehman Brothers coincided with a sharp decline in Fed Funds lending and borrowing. Banks not only shifted to using the Federal Reserve’s [Discount Window](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/discountwindow.asp) (the facility it uses to perform its “lender of last resort” capabilities) to obtain Fed Funds, but the U.S, central bank’s QE (quantitative easing) replaced a large chunk of the bank’s Treasury holdings with reserve balances. A paradigm shift had unfolded. Since the megabanks, though primarily JPMorgan, had absorbed most of the Fed’s QE purchases, they had become swamped in reserves, never needing to borrow in the Fed Funds market. The smaller regional banks, once primary Fed Funds lenders, were now the ones in deficit. Still, even they had enough reserves to avoid borrowing in the interbank market.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!W59w!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9975802d-d2b7-4e24-a386-51280cad4706_1402x963.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!W59w!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9975802d-d2b7-4e24-a386-51280cad4706_1402x963.png)

_bank balance sheets exhibit no interbank lending activity (Fed Funds sold/purchased)_

Still, regardless of banks being awash with reserves, the killing blow for the Fed Funds market was yet to come. In response to the GFC, the global regulatory establishment, the [BIS](https://www.bis.org/) (the Bank for International Settlements), began devising a new regulatory regime for banks, dubbed the [Basel III Accords](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/basell-iii.asp#:~:text=Basel%20III%20is%20an%20international,being%20implemented%20as%20of%202022.). The bull market in stringent regulation had officially commenced.

From 2009, the Basel Framework — as it’s now known — crushed volumes in the Fed Funds market even further. First, its [leverage ratio](https://www.bis.org/publ/bcbs270.htm#:~:text=Basel%20III's%20leverage%20ratio%20is,is%20expressed%20as%20a%20percentage.) forced banks to increase the amount of capital (i.e. [common equity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_stock) and earnings retained from usual business) held against their assets, in order to remain increasingly resilient to large losses during crises. This forced banks to prioritize the most cost-effective trades, and interbank lending was at the bottom of their list. Moreover, the major participants in Fed Funds, global systemically important banks known as GSIBs, were beholden to even higher requirements. [GSIB surcharges](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/ccyb_gsib/index.htm) further limited their abilities to make markets in interbank loans.

[

![Basel Committee's Revisions to the Basel III Leverage Ratio](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!m7LZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6f0df2b-560c-43b5-9ed3-983df65a5a4c_720x396.jpeg "Basel Committee's Revisions to the Basel III Leverage Ratio")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!m7LZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6f0df2b-560c-43b5-9ed3-983df65a5a4c_720x396.jpeg)

_Source: Harvard Law_

Banks not only had to hold a certain amount of capital for almost every asset on their balance sheets. After the introduction of RWAs (risk-weighted assets), assets were deemed different with respect to the risks they posed. The higher the risk (weighting), the more capital that was mandated. From interest rate derivatives to interbank loans, every asset on a bank’s book had been risk-assessed and weighted by regulators. With Fed Funds, for instance, a bank simply lending to another bank applied a sizeable 20% risk weighting to a trade. Basel had even indirectly outlawed some assets, especially highly speculative investments. Banks were forced to assign risk weightings of up to 400% to speculative equity positions, plus a risk weighting of 250% to all other equity holdings. Last year, some officials [proposed](https://www.linkedin.com/pulse/basel-30-1250-risk-weight-cryptoassets-like-bitcoin-berquet#:~:text=Under%20the%20new%20guidelines%2C%20banks,or%20other%20cryptoasset%20they%20hold.) a whopping 1250% for crypto-related investments.

But the leverage ratio and RWAs were only two of many mechanisms that regulators implemented. Next, the LCR (Liquidity Coverage Ratio) further disincentivized banks from lending to one another via Fed Funds. The LCR forced banks to maintain, not lend out, a portfolio of “[HQLA](https://www.bis.org/basel_framework/chapter/LCR/30.htm#:~:text=Assets%20are%20considered%20to%20be,or%20no%20loss%20of%20value.)” (high-quality liquid assets comprising mostly bank reserves, plus a smaller portion of other “safe assets”), which held enough liquidity to withstand a month’s worth of customer outflows (withdrawals).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ph8K!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2ae8b4ad-ae34-4289-b6c7-a27860bddb00_4300x1394.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ph8K!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2ae8b4ad-ae34-4289-b6c7-a27860bddb00_4300x1394.png)

Alongside the LCR, regulators also created the NSFR (Net Stable Funding Ratio), which forced banks to reduce their liquidity risk by replacing “flightier” deposits (like those of institutional investors) with more retail deposits and longer-term debt, forms of funding that Basel’s architects deemed the most resilient to bank runs. The inflow of less-flighty reserves they obtained from attracting a larger retail deposit base further removed the need and incentive for them to lend in the Fed Funds market.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8UuW!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F420250bb-a50c-497b-90a6-60755301f9d5_3587x1442.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8UuW!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F420250bb-a50c-497b-90a6-60755301f9d5_3587x1442.png)

If that wasn’t enough, U.S. regulators then added an extra “non-risk weighted” ratio: the SLR (supplementary leverage ratio), which deemed reserves and U.S Treasuries just as toxic as mortgage-backed securities and credit default swaps. After the SLR went live, there was, beyond doubt, no space on banks’ balance sheets to facilitate trades with low profitability, like borrowing and lending Fed Funds.

[

![US G-SIB Leverage Surcharge and Basel III Leverage Ratio](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZCP7!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg "US G-SIB Leverage Surcharge and Basel III Leverage Ratio")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZCP7!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg)

_Source: Harvard Law_

Slowly but surely, over the last decade, assets deemed too risky, including unsecured interbank loans like Fed Funds, have been indirectly outlawed from banks. They are now incentivized to only use safer forms of funding, like retail deposits and repo (cash loans secured against U.S. Treasuries and other forms of high-quality collateral). With interbank lending, banks have nearly deserted markets originally designed for them. Today, all that remains of the Fed Funds markets is an arbitrage trade between two uncommon entities: the New York branches of foreign banks (banks operating globally that were founded outside the United States) and the Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs), government-sponsored enterprises that aim to provide cheap mortgage [credit](https://www.fhlb-of.com/ofweb_userWeb/pageBuilder/home) to U.S. citizens and businesses.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!E9wR!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff4796c91-7d66-4135-8242-929892a6d845_7556x4515.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!E9wR!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff4796c91-7d66-4135-8242-929892a6d845_7556x4515.png)

Not subject to the same stringent regulations as U.S.-based banks, foreign banks can engage in arbitrage in the Fed Funds market. Their goal is to profit from borrowing reserves at a cheaper rate than “IORB” (interest on reserve balances), the rate the Fed pays on reserve balances. They borrow from the only significant lender of reserves: Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs), which have access to the Federal Reserve System. Unlike foreign banks, FHLBs can’t earn interest from the Fed on their balances, though they can — and do — lend reserves. This key difference enables a Fed Funds arbitrage trade. FHLBs lend reserves at the Effective Federal Funds Rate (EFFR) to foreign banks, who then “deposit” those reserves at the Fed, earning IORB. The spread between IORB and EFFR is the profit, which presently earns foreign banks 0.07%.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9sQJ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F541a6173-99c4-4c1f-8144-077b8a44ba2f_6731x3487.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9sQJ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F541a6173-99c4-4c1f-8144-077b8a44ba2f_6731x3487.png)

_click to enlarge (best viewed in a desktop browser)_

A typical FHLB will borrow money from government MMFs (money market funds that invest mainly corporate and institutional investor cash balances into government-related securities, like U.S. Treasuries and [agency MBS](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/a/agency-mbs-purchase.asp)) by issuing [agency discount notes](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/discountnote.asp#:~:text=A%20discount%20note%20is%20a,to%20one%20year%20in%20length.) — known informally as “agency discos” — at a rate lower than it will eventually lend out in the Fed Funds market. The FHLB will then use the money raised from agency discount notes to lend to entities (primarily foreign banks) in the Federal Funds market at [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) (the Effective Federal Funds Rate), currently 5.33%. Foreign banks then borrow Fed Funds at EFFR and park them at the Fed, earning the spread between IORB and EFFR (5.40% - 5.33% = 0.07%).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4dxw!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1200cd61-656f-41c4-a3b2-940e749479f1_7004x2449.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4dxw!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1200cd61-656f-41c4-a3b2-940e749479f1_7004x2449.png)

_click to enlarge (best viewed in a desktop browser)_

Other than a tiny number of small banks falling short of reserves, the only other significant trade in the Fed Funds market are Eurodollar transactions. In a world where the U.S. dollar is a global currency, it makes sense to clear foreign and domestic dollar transactions between banks using one currency (Fed Funds) in one system (the Federal Reserve System). Subsequently, the Eurodollar and Fed Funds markets are seen as identical and fungible, as long as the Fed’s implicit global promise to provide both onshore and offshore dollars persists.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!IJZ3!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F06a17fdb-4f7e-4d5f-bc2e-71cad36cb56b_9039x3220.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!IJZ3!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F06a17fdb-4f7e-4d5f-bc2e-71cad36cb56b_9039x3220.png)

_click to enlarge (best viewed in a desktop browser)_

Right now, the onshore Fed Funds market is so deserted that the market for settling Eurodollar (offshore dollar) transactions is greater than Fed Funds borrowing and lending activities. The Basel Framework’s LCR enforces global reserve requirements for New York branches of foreign banks .i.e under LCR, Fed Funds, not offshore dollar deposits, act as the settlement medium for Eurodollar transactions. All these factors result in Eurodollar and Fed Funds rates trading almost in line.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qKm_!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F407328cd-4702-4c88-8c0b-081549788e2b_12314x9158.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qKm_!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F407328cd-4702-4c88-8c0b-081549788e2b_12314x9158.png)

As long as the Big Three prevails, bank reserves will remain ample in the banking system, with the Fed Funds market doomed to a low-volume existence of enabling arbitrage between irregular entities and clearing Eurodollar transactions. An attempt to return to a scarce reserve system is underway, as the Federal Reserve reduces its balance sheet via QT (quantitative tightening). Still, little progress has been made to return to “normal levels” of reserves. It’s evident, for now, that an ample reserve system and a semi-lifeless Fed Funds market are here to stay.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_
