# The Stealth Liquidity Squeeze - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> U.S. Treasury officials have reluctantly deployed covert tightening

---
After easing financial conditions via a series of covert actions, monetary leaders can no longer pretend to tighten. The dark side of Quantitative Tightening (QT) has been unleashed, not by the Fed itself, but by the U.S. Treasury. The Stealth Liquidity Squeeze™ is here.

In early winter at the start of 2022, the Federal Reserve announced it was undertaking the monumental task of unwinding its $9 trillion balance sheet. The U.S. central bank’s second Quantitative Tightening (QT) program had officially commenced. This coincided with the Fed’s full-blown tightening cycle, including sharp rate hikes that shook markets globally. Anticipating peak liquidity and growth, plus the death of the “inflation is transitory” narrative, risk assets — once powered by a huge speculative frenzy — sold off sharply. Subsequently, the idea that QT was a major drag on asset prices had been cemented.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QHg7!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F90d3ed02-89e2-4fac-aa28-cece96d9b7e2_3008x2226.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QHg7!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F90d3ed02-89e2-4fac-aa28-cece96d9b7e2_3008x2226.png)

As months and quarters passed, however, QT took a backseat. Slowing growth and intense rate hikes were blamed as the primary causes behind the popping of the greatest market mania on record. The Federal Reserve’s “inflation rug pull” had resulted in a slow, protracted 45% decline in equities, a once-in-a-lifetime selloff in government bonds, and the collapse of many crypto empires. Despite the decline, the stability of the system persisted, but only until September 2022.

The [near-failure](https://enterprise.press/stories/2022/10/03/explaining-the-uks-bond-market-meltdown-82820/) of U.K’s bond market and the following intervention by the Bank of England marked the bottom for global liquidity. Still, whether intentional or not, monetary authorities globally, but especially the Fed and U.S. Treasury, maintained a tightening stance publicly. Yet behind the scenes, they continued to supply enough liquidity to stave off any crisis. After multiple episodes, from a banking panic to a debt ceiling fiasco, they unofficially gave up on enforcing any meaningful tightening. “Stealth QE” was the new policy.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5aHS!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F058f4213-5508-4aed-b9d6-c1d7b93984ab_1987x1750.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5aHS!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F058f4213-5508-4aed-b9d6-c1d7b93984ab_1987x1750.png)

As 2023 arrived, the debt ceiling quarrel forced the U.S. Treasury to draw down almost its entire bank balance in the [TGA](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/WTREGEN) (Treasury General Account), pushing reserves back into the system, thus once again offsetting QT’s ability to hinder liquidity. What’s more, the U.S. Treasury continued to issue only short-term government debt, further hindering QT’s impact to quash risk sentiment. Even after the Silicon Valley Bank collapse emerged, the U.S. government’s bailout of depositors and regional bank rescue programs only further reinforced the idea that monetary leaders were willing to do anything to ease financial conditions, still without an official pivot. By implementing the [BTFP](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F974a55d3-80fc-4aa8-bd8e-bbdfbc566e47_2413x1637.png) (Bank Term Funding Program), the Fed signaled to the market that they were willing to remove as much interest rate risk as needed. Bond volatility decreased rapidly, which rippled into credit and other risk assets, juicing markets further. With liquidity sustained, the U.S. economy grew more resilient than most economists had expected.

Fast forward to today, liquidity has remained so abundant that even the resolution of the debt ceiling and the ensuing “[TGA refill](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3521113a-6a5e-42a6-bbda-e71215af0a00_3774x2940.png)” have failed to subdue risk asset prices. But the tide is now turning. After a series of false alarms, the Fed’s QT has begun to dampen risk appetite. With Janet Yellen issuing a sea of riskier bonds into the market, it’s the U.S. Treasury’s latest actions, not the Fed’s, that have begun to impair risk assets. TQT (Tighter Quantitative Tightening) has been activated, unleashing a stealth tightening effect on markets that most liquidity indicators won’t detect. What does that mean in practice? It’s time to go deeper into the mechanics.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cJM6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6a8b99e-7db7-4df6-872d-b6d916193d18_6648x7338.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cJM6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6a8b99e-7db7-4df6-872d-b6d916193d18_6648x7338.png)

_click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)_

With normal QT (quantitative tightening), the U.S. government reduces the Fed’s balance sheet by a set amount every month, presently $95 billion, by selling new Treasuries to the private sector and using the proceeds to pay off maturing bonds on the Fed’s balance sheet. The U.S. central bank lets the bonds mature off its books while also destroying the same amount in bank reserves. The end result is not a decrease in the number of bonds but a loss of reserves and deposits, thus reducing “liquidity” in the banking system.

Even so, the Fed does not possess the most effective QT weapon. In past episodes, the Fed’s QT alone failed to bring down asset prices, exposing an inconvenient truth: The U.S. Treasury and its officials have always dictated the tightening impact of QT, not the Federal Reserve. When the U.S. Treasury chooses to issue a large number of bills, markets absorb the supply with ease, no matter how abundant. Because the yield on short-term Treasuries equals the risk-free rate, market participants are taking on zero additional risk, and thus don’t need to adjust their risk profile by selling riskier assets to compensate.

But now that TQT has commenced, U.S. Treasury Sec. Janet Yellen has increased the tenacity of QT by releasing more Treasury notes and bonds, securities with longer maturities and more duration risk, into the market. When participants buy these bonds, they will either use cash balances, borrow money, or sell other investments. Since purchasing Treasury bills carries minimal duration risk, market players will most likely use cash or borrow money. But with notes and bonds, it’s different. The added interest rate risk of Treasury notes and bonds forces market players to reassess their risk tolerance, potentially selling riskier assets to counteract the increased risk they are bearing.

Today, the increase in net issuance of notes and bonds, hence duration risk, remains minimal. But markets are forward-looking. Ever since the Treasury [announced](https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/jy1671) huge increases in “coupon issuance,” jargon for issuing Treasury notes and bonds, in the coming quarters, market participants have been frontrunning the impact of TQT, by selling risk assets at the fastest pace this year. The U.S. Treasury is set to increase bonds with added duration risk by ~$185 billion in Q3, rising to ~$339 billion in Q4.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zBD5!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F624fc9f4-75c5-4b9b-afc7-c61f0fe31cc6_831x477.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zBD5!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F624fc9f4-75c5-4b9b-afc7-c61f0fe31cc6_831x477.png)

Despite all this, we’re told that the Fed holds all the cards when it comes to tightening financial conditions, and that the U.S. Treasury is a completely independent entity. Yet both have tried, indirectly and covertly, to boost liquidity while reiterating a tightening stance on the surface. Now, though, they have reached their limitations. The world demands that U.S. monetary officials issue riskier bonds into the market. QT is gradually turning into TQT, allowing quantitative tightening to possess enough power to curb risk appetite. Finally, after almost a year of muted impact on liquidity and sentiment, the monetary plumbing has produced a genuine bearish catalyst.

But while the economy remains resilient, the job market remains tight, and the Fed’s rate hikes fail to have their desired outcome, TQT alone won’t be enough to slay the stock market’s natural tendency to rally further. Risk assets won’t rise as smoothly and easily as before. Still, it will likely take more disorder to terminate the rip-roaring rally in risk assets we’ve witnessed so far this year. Since markets have already front-ran the effects of a stronger, more potent QT, something else has to give.

Minus TQT, most monetary leaders’ stealth easing programs remain in effect, while economic resilience and adequate liquidity have persisted longer than most have anticipated. If this continues, markets may only transition into a choppy period, not a serious correction. As other asset classes continue on their present trajectory, risk assets are likely to experience mild turbulence. The Great Chop™, as it will come to be known, is here, and it’s only starting to take shape.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Rise of the Secured Standard - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a primer on dealers .i.e the monetary plumbers

---
Every day in financial markets, over $11 trillion in dollar funding transactions take place, fueling the world’s ever-increasing demand for trade and commerce within America’s [global security alliance](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-eurodollar-system). While internationally active banks and hedge funds tap trillions in funding from global money pools — cash balances of institutions and corporate giants — via [repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-money-markets) and money market funds ([MMFs](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/m/money-marketfund.asp)), large corporations issue short-term debt in commercial paper markets to fund their operations. These transactions, of course, provide only a glimpse into the exotic dealings that occur in the dollar system daily. But what every trade has in common is the reliance on a particular entity: dealers A.K.A the monetary plumbers. Without them, the U.S. empire’s liquidity machine would break down.

Ever since America disarmed financial tensions in the post-war period, virtually all prominent financial institutions from every major nation launched thriving dealer businesses. Not only that, but they assembled trading desks to make markets in just about every dollar funding instrument, from [FX swaps](https://corporatefinanceinstitute.com/resources/derivatives/foreign-currency-swap/) to certificates of deposit ([CDs](https://corporatefinanceinstitute.com/resources/wealth-management/certificate-of-deposit-cd/#:~:text=A%20certificate%20of%20deposit%20(CD)%20refers%20to%20a%20financial%20product,or%20risk%20paying%20a%20penalty)) to repo agreements. Megabanks like JPMorgan even ran trading desks for lending and borrowing bank reserves, with brokers of Federal Funds (the official name for reserves traded in the Federal Reserve System) stationed in New York helping them source interbank loans and [Eurodollars](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-eurodollar-system).

But with regulators, in response to the GFC (Great Financial Crisis) of 2008, implementing _Conks’_ Big Three™ — [The Dodd-Frank Act](https://www.cftc.gov/LawRegulation/DoddFrankAct/index.htm) (hindering banks’ risk-taking abilities), [The Basel Framework](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/basell-iii.asp#:~:text=Basel%20III%20is%20an%20international,being%20implemented%20as%20of%202022.) (discouraging systemic risk and excessive leverage), and the SEC’s [money market reform](https://investor.vanguard.com/investor-resources-education/mutual-funds/money-market-reform#:~:text=Money%20market%20reform%20is%20a,retail%2C%20government%2C%20and%20institutional.) (substituting riskier dollar funding sources with safer alternatives), the “secured standard” has arisen.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KI60!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F18b974be-df4b-4f50-948e-3c5cdffb9a06_5834x4796.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KI60!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F18b974be-df4b-4f50-948e-3c5cdffb9a06_5834x4796.png)

Subsequently, most unsecured dollar markets have begun to decline into irrelevancy, along with the dealers and trading desks that fueled their ascent. As the secured standard has evolved and expanded over the past decade, most activity from its dying counterpart has been transferred over to repo (secured cash loans) and securities lending, which combine to form the Secured Dollar Funding Complex (SDFC). To understand the financial plumbing of the future, demystifying the dealers that supply liquidity to the SDFC is vital. Securities dealers, however, are quite intricate. It’s time to dive into the world of the monetary plumbers.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!R_ri!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0a9c4c66-f617-4c34-8129-6689e60226e1_2220x1274.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!R_ri!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0a9c4c66-f617-4c34-8129-6689e60226e1_2220x1274.png)

Today’s secured standard is an extremely liquid and efficient marketplace for lending and borrowing dollars backed by collateral such as U.S Treasuries. But for this to happen, securities dealers must supply liquidity by running “matched books,” which resemble a typical balance sheet. Dealers borrow dollars (secured against Treasury collateral) using a [repo](https://twitter.com/concodanomics/status/1667603294825615362?s=20) and lend these out (once again, secured against Treasury collateral) in a [reverse repo](https://twitter.com/concodanomics/status/1667603294825615362?s=20), earning a profit from the spread they charge — the difference between the rate they lend and borrow.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZNyO!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F90bae932-293c-4473-a085-9cf47f7be4e0_2781x2388.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZNyO!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F90bae932-293c-4473-a085-9cf47f7be4e0_2781x2388.png)

The incentive for market participants operating within the secured standard to pay dealers is that they are willing to pay for consistent access to liquidity. If liquidity were freely available and abundant, dealers would not be in business. They ensure that liquidity remains ample, however, they also guarantee that the value of the securities being traded will always be mispriced. The price paid for plentiful liquidity in dollar funding markets is the persistent distortion of value — via securities dealers charging spreads. This makes dealers’ “spread trading” businesses highly lucrative but also extremely competitive. Accordingly, the dealer industry suffers from a tremendous amount of churn. When profits dry up — implying that the spread between borrowing and lending has grown thin — the size of the industry becomes volatile. Unlike large dealers, the less prominent liquidity providers usually don’t survive for long.

Most dealers have therefore realized that it’s useful to diversify by also functioning as a broker (hence the broker-dealer terminology). Since providing liquidity requires time and expertise, such as solving clearing and settlement issues, building relationships with counterparties, gaining access to exchanges, plus acquiring market knowledge, intelligence, and negotiation skills, brokers are indispensable middlemen. They represent clients to fill their orders, while the reverse isn’t feasible.

That’s because most market participants, even most professionals, can’t be relied on to clear and settle trades themselves. They could refuse to acknowledge or settle trades, run of out funds to honor trades, or even simply forget that certain trades exist. Over time, as with most other ecosystems, human traders put their faith in a third party or two to eliminate these potential issues and associated frictions. Market participants also realized that brokers only wanted to onboard and retain trustworthy clients, increasing the attractiveness of their centralized systems for clearing and settlement. What’s more, because brokers spent ever more time and money solving these issues, they ultimately lowered the cost of liquidity.

On top of providing brokerage services and liquidity via their matched books, dealers run a simultaneous speculative book, predicting price changes in the assets they usually intermediate when mispricings grow evident. In highly competitive markets, dealers benefit from running speculative books primarily because sometimes these are necessary to remain in business. While spread traders profit from funding at the bid and lending at the ask, speculative traders profit using the dealer’s capital.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Zi7Y!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4aabfbf4-961f-4b54-b63c-496753b9a1b7_960x720.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Zi7Y!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4aabfbf4-961f-4b54-b63c-496753b9a1b7_960x720.png)

_Source: Jack Treynor_

Dealers tend to excel within what’s known as the inside spread, the range between the highest bid and lowest ask. They remain vulnerable, however, in the outside spread, where losses tend to arise because of so-called informed traders. When it comes to the outside spread, despite access to a sea of information and intelligence, dealers are considered to be uninformed traders, while their clients such as hedge funds and investment banks are said to be informed. Dealers specialize in market making, but their customers — who’ve usually dedicated their time to spotting mispricings and analyzing the assets they’re trading — specialize in trading the outside spread. Dealers are thus more susceptible to adverse selection: their counterparties, more often than not, have access to information even more superior than theirs.

All these functions coalesce to form the average dealer operating within the secured standard. Running a matched and speculative book, dealers operate several different desks, the number of which depends on the complexity and diversification of their operations. Their prime broker(age) deals mostly with hedge fund business — like borrowing to short, while their derivatives and financing desks oversee trades with other entities. These desks also handle the management and flow of collateral and communication with potential third parties involved in the market-making process, such as the [FICC](https://www.dtcc.com/about/businesses-and-subsidiaries/ficc) ([central clearing](https://www.bis.org/publ/qtrpdf/r_qt1512g.pdf)) and the [Bank of New York Mellon](https://www.bnymellon.com/emea/en/about-us/about-bny-mellon.html) ([triparty custodian](https://www.bnymellon.com/content/dam/bnymellon/documents/pdf/articles/triparty-repo-brochure.pdf.coredownload.pdf)).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!HiBT!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F858aea3a-8e92-4318-b075-acee764c1461_5519x3581.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!HiBT!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F858aea3a-8e92-4318-b075-acee764c1461_5519x3581.png)

Securities dealers don’t only connect cash borrowers and cash lenders but unite collateral lenders and collateral borrowers. Introducing the “securities lending” market. The main objective of securities lending is to obtain particular securities (by pledging other securities) in order to trade in a particular market. Financial institutions swap securities with one another to gain access to areas of finance (primarily distinct areas of repo), which require a certain calibre of security. This process is known as collateral upgrading and downgrading. For instance, a prime MMF (money market fund) may look to borrow cash in the triparty repo market. But nobody is willing to lend cash against the corporate bonds that the prime MMF holds. The prime MMF can contact one of its counterparties in the securities lending market to “upgrade” its collateral i.e. swapping its highly-rated corporate bonds for another party’s U.S. Treasuries temporarily for a fee. Now that the prime MMF has U.S. Treasuries in its possession, it can participate in the triparty repo market, obtaining a secured cash loan.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4rMK!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Febe13c3b-b2ee-43ea-bd30-5bec194079d9_2265x450.jpeg)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4rMK!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Febe13c3b-b2ee-43ea-bd30-5bec194079d9_2265x450.jpeg)

Source: OFR

In the secured standard, sovereign states via their central banks play more of an overarching role in markets. With securities lending, it’s no different, especially after central banks engaged in epic asset purchases during the GFC and Covid-19. Every day, the Fed’s primary dealers [borrow](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/desk-operations/securities-lending) securities from its balance sheet to fuel liquidity in the secured standard — the same liquidity the Fed had removed by acquiring a large portfolio of assets. What’s more, if collateral — for some reason — becomes scarce, private sector dealers can rely on the Fed’s securities lending arm as (yet another) dealer of last resort.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!52z5!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fbucketeer-e05bbc84-baa3-437e-9518-adb32be77984.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F47592b9b-d8ac-43bf-b717-e87e2756c7bc_2619x1433.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!52z5!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fbucketeer-e05bbc84-baa3-437e-9518-adb32be77984.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F47592b9b-d8ac-43bf-b717-e87e2756c7bc_2619x1433.png)

Out of the gigantic ~$4.5 trillion in Treasuries sitting on its balance sheet, the Fed loans out billions in specific issues daily to primary dealers like Goldman Sachs and Nomura through its Securities Lending [facility](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/domestic-market-operations/monetary-policy-implementation/securities-lending). When primary dealers, or one of their clients, find an opportunity worth paying an [extra](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/sec_faq.html) 0.05%, they will borrow U.S. Treasuries from the U.S. central bank’s [SOMA](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/soma-holdings) (System Open Market Account) portfolio. Typically, these entities go on to charge a higher spread to hedge funds looking to source specific securities.

Subsequently, the Fed’s mammoth balance sheet, once considered a bug, has been turned into a prominent feature, a common occurence in the secured standard era. With this and other “secured features” emerging from the unsecured standard’s demise, demand for repo and securities lending will continue to prosper. And the major beneficiaries will be the dealers who make it all possible.

That’s a wrap! We’ll cover more about dealers in upcoming pieces and in the book. If you have any queries or need clarification on any minor details, leave a comment below.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks subscriber!**_

# The Treasury Market Basis Trade - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a dubious yet vital mechanism is binding U.S. bond markets together

---
A risky, elaborate trade, one infamous for blowing up everytime, is once again growing in popularity inside the world’s most systemic bond market. When turmoil inevitably re-emerges, only the Federal Reserve’s [volatility suppressor](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression) will come to the rescue. The Treasury Market Unwind™ is looming.

In early 2018, a string of events formed an exploit in America’s sovereign debt market. Following a large issuance of U.S. Treasuries, a fall in foreign demand, and regulatory reforms, asset managers — pension funds, mutual funds, and insurance companies — began to shift out of [cash bonds](https://www.marketwatch.com/investing/bond/tmubmusd10y?countrycode=bx) and into long positions of their [associated](https://www.cmegroup.com/markets/interest-rates/us-treasury/10-year-us-treasury-note.html) Treasury futures. This reallocation was so intense that prices of Treasury futures and of those in the underlying cash market began to diverge. Noticing this deviation and subsequent arbitrage opportunity, hedge funds increasingly took the opposite side of asset managers’ positions. The Treasury cash-futures “basis trade” was re-emerging.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oa-i!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdc80a8ea-87fd-4e61-8f26-9f886a8130ce_7072x4269.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oa-i!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdc80a8ea-87fd-4e61-8f26-9f886a8130ce_7072x4269.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

By going “long the basis,” which meant simultaneously shorting Treasury futures and buying the securities underlying these contracts in the cash market, hedge funds — primarily [relative-value funds](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/relativevalue-funds.asp#:~:text=Relative%20value%20funds%20are%20typically,related%20securities%20they%20consider%20overvalued.) — had high odds of profiting from what was deemed a nearly risk-free [arbitrage](https://www.investopedia.com/ask/answers/what-is-arbitrage/#:~:text=Arbitrage%20describes%20the%20act%20of,difference%20in%20cost%20per%20share.). Futures prices kept rising markedly above the price of their underlying Treasury securities, allowing traders to buy bonds at a discount to what they’d receive when delivering these securities into futures contracts. The basis, the difference in price between cash and futures (minus financing costs), was the profit.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PgGc!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2b7b7d22-be8c-429d-84c4-e7d172a245e1_1662x1992.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PgGc!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2b7b7d22-be8c-429d-84c4-e7d172a245e1_1662x1992.png)

_Source: OFR_

But although this trade offered nearly risk-free returns, the resulting profits were lackluster. For hedge funds to achieve significant enough returns, they had to employ immense amounts of leverage. To execute these highly leveraged trades, hedge funds called upon the bilateral repo market — the market for cash loans secured against specific securities. By borrowing cash against the Treasuries they would use to deliver into futures contracts, hedge funds only needed to commit a fraction of capital to finance basis trade positions. The amount of leverage hedge fund traders achieved was determined by the repo market they used to finance Treasury purchases.

Hedge funds borrowed in either [uncleared](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-repo-market-blindspot), [cleared](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership), or [sponsored](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) bilateral repo markets, each dominated by leveraged players funding various exotic trades. While cleared and sponsored repo (which is the same as cleared but entities gain access to trading through a third-party sponsor) offered greater transparency and lower counterparty risk, uncleared repo markets delivered superior leverage. Cash lenders in each repo market lent money equal to the value of the Treasury securities the cash borrower (i.e. the hedge fund) pledged, minus a haircut: a percentage the lender subtracted from the security’s value to protect itself against adverse market movements. In the shadowy depths of the uncleared repo market, haircuts fell to as low as 0%, allowing for much higher leverage than cleared and sponsored repo markets.

Subsequently, uncleared bilateral repo — known officially as [NCCBR](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-repo-market-blindspot) (non-centrally-cleared bilateral repo) — swamped cleared repo in size and activity, with an [extra](https://x.com/not_concoda/status/1701575198518956412?s=20) ~$1.4 trillion in outstanding transactions. Soon enough by 2019, hedge funds were regularly borrowing up to a hundred times the capital they committed to Treasury basis trades, achieving in most cases more than 99:1 leverage. For every one dollar of capital, they borrowed a whopping $99 in repo to finance trades.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!TZNZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F408cc63c-3289-4f17-a72b-7490513282c7_5481x3213.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!TZNZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F408cc63c-3289-4f17-a72b-7490513282c7_5481x3213.png)

As 2020 approached, short futures positions had soared by over 100%, with hedge funds making up ~73% of the volume. Billions upon billions of dollars had been allocated to highly leveraged basis trades, on what was perceived to be a low-risk, high-return wager. The trade’s major vulnerabilities were only exposed to the fiercest of black swan events, but as it turned out, the COVID market panic was around the corner. Just as the basis trade was rippling into the mainstream, the Treasury Market Unwind™ had commenced.

As the COVID financial meltdown arose in March 2020, the defects arising from enormous interventions and radical responses to the GFC (Great Financial Crisis) became apparent for all to witness. Before regulators implemented an [ocean](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc15acdf5-5341-4d93-8a07-c821016fbb37_5958x5674.png) of rules and regulations, from the Dodd-Frank Act to the Basel Framework, the structure of the system created a default flight to safety into the U.S. Treasury market during a crisis. But after a decade of moving from an unsecured to a [secured standard](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-rise-of-the-secured-standard), markets had now already absorbed a large amount of Treasuries as a safeguard, the backbone of the new regulatory regime. The monetary plumbers and intermediaries of the financial system had already flipped from net short to net long Treasuries, even before the crisis had unfolded.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!akv0!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fcced17e2-3044-4c08-b8a9-18565958c9cc_2058x1651.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!akv0!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fcced17e2-3044-4c08-b8a9-18565958c9cc_2058x1651.png)

_Source: New York Fed_

Thus, as the COVID turmoil transpired, it became evident that the traditional flight of safety into Treasuries had already taken place. The new flight to safety in times of crisis had become a dash for cash .i.e cold, hard U.S. dollars. As pandemic fears worsened, Treasury market volatility spiked and bid-ask spreads widened. Investors who never expected to need to liquidate Treasuries, mainly foreign central banks and U.S money market funds, suddenly dumped their holdings en masse. _Conks’_ infamous [illiquidity spiral](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-great-sovereign-debt-intervention) had arisen.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!t9pu!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7f21b6f3-f951-4ac4-a2ca-b64f412bc506_1856x1218.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!t9pu!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7f21b6f3-f951-4ac4-a2ca-b64f412bc506_1856x1218.png)

_Source: OFR_

The illiquidity created via these fire-sales of Treasuries rippled through the financial system, enough to finally contest the Treasury futures basis trade’s vulnerabilities: margin risk (Treasury futures and cash prices move against the leveraged trader’s position, prompting margin calls) and rollover risk (the interest rate to rollover repo loans skyrockets).

Traders financing their positions using repo loans rolled over on an overnight basis had to unwind a now unaffordable trade. Meanwhile, those hit by large increases in margin requirements were hit with margin calls. By early March, the IRR (implied repo rate) — another name for the profit on the trade — had dropped below the cost of repo financing, making the long-basis trade unprofitable for many.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nlWO!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc2745b5c-00e8-4ed4-be23-3610d6ed21d6_1347x1582.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nlWO!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc2745b5c-00e8-4ed4-be23-3610d6ed21d6_1347x1582.png)

_the long basis trade becoming unprofitable during the COVID crash (with bill rates falling below repo borrowing costs) — Source: OFR_

But before a mass unwind of basis trades could cause serious damage, exacerbating an illiquidity spiral, the Federal Reserve’s “[volatility suppressor](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression)” came to the rescue. By merely announcing it would [pledge](https://www.nytimes.com/2020/03/23/business/economy/coronavirus-fed-bond-buying.html) to purchase an unlimited amount of Treasuries, calm re-emerged. Dealers and other monetary plumbers, once scared hoarders, recommenced making markets in Treasuries, prompting the basis trade to not only become profitable again but even provide liquidity as markets healed. All the Federal Reserve had to do to revive market sentiment was announce it was willing to step in.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1KCl!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0b74653f-7c2f-4645-8c4c-f491c3590dd6_3263x1140.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1KCl!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0b74653f-7c2f-4645-8c4c-f491c3590dd6_3263x1140.png)

_recent hedge fund repo volume and repo borrowing against treasuries (left) - Leveraged Funds Shorts TTF data (right) - click to enlarge | Source: New York Fed_

Fast forward to today, and a recent [Fed study](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/recent-developments-in-hedge-funds-treasury-futures-and-repo-positions-20230830.html) has revealed convincing evidence that the Treasury cash-futures basis trade is not only back but booming. Familiar futures positioning and borrowing patterns in DVP repo markets (jargon for bilateral repo) have started to gain momentum. Like before the COVID crash occurred, the basis trade is likely helping to bind the Treasury, cash, and repo markets firmly together.

So when disruption arises in the U.S. Treasury market once more, the Fed will be on higher alert than ever before, watching and waiting to fire up its volatility suppressor to prevent any Treasury market turmoil. The only major disorder to arise from the next near-miss will be outrage over the Fed’s ever-growing power in markets, especially their eagerness to step in at the first sign of danger. Bailouts but also political backlash are now both guaranteed.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_
