# Demystifying the Treasury Market - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a deep dive on the most systemically important market globally

---
Ever since the rise of the U.S. empire, the Treasury market has grown to become its monetary backbone. America’s sovereign debt has not only been used to price trillions of dollars in financial assets but store trillions of dollars in wealth. What’s more, with the recent rise of the [secured standard](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-rise-of-the-secured-standard), Treasuries are slowly backing most of the [elaborate chains](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) powering global finance. Still, despite its dominance, the U.S. Treasury market has remained relatively obscure. Barely any elements of its plumbing have been willingly disclosed in detail.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1047!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F50f0f2af-ba5f-4ca5-976b-444005014beb_5366x4246.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1047!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F50f0f2af-ba5f-4ca5-976b-444005014beb_5366x4246.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

That is, however, until now. In this comprehensive deep dive, we’ll demystify the inner workings of the U.S. Treasury market, both past and present, unveiling the history, mechanics, and monetary plumbing of the most systemically important market globally. Let’s dive in.

It’s hard to imagine a world without electronic securities, but only half a century earlier, the vast majority of U.S. Treasuries were issued in physical form. In the early 1960s, paperless records of sovereign debt securities had yet to be invented, rendering the concept of Treasury market plumbing nonexistent.

Instead, the U.S. government issued bonds in two physical formats: bearer bonds and registered bonds. A bearer bond consisted of a physical piece of paper with the U.S. government’s pledge to pay back the principal, plus a row of detachable coupons, each granting a claim on one interest payment on a specific date. Since the U.S. Treasury had no method of tracking ownership, whoever held the bearer security in physical form received its cashflows. On the days that the bond paid out interest or matured, the holder contacted the U.S. Treasury to obtain payment. If the holder wanted to sell the bond, they simply transferred ownership via physical delivery and received cash in return, sometimes without ever having to contact state officials. Subsequently, loss and theft of Treasury securities in bearer bond form became a regular occurrence.

Meanwhile, with registered bonds, the name and address of the holder were listed on the physical security. Only they were entitled to the principal and interest payments from the U.S. government. Registered bonds possessed an engraved certificate, not only identifying the holder but also acting as a mechanism to change ownership. Because the name and address of the bondholder were also kept on record at the U.S. Treasury, a registered bond owner looking to sell would fill out the re-registration instructions on the back and send it to state officials for processing. Officials then issued a new certificate inscribed with the new owner’s details, which were used to update the Treasury’s records. This enabled registered bonds to be replaced if lost, stolen, or ruined, making safekeeping and transportation relatively painless.

Regardless of these superior qualities, however, transferring ownership of registered bonds was a tiresome process. Institutional investors favored keeping U.S. Treasuries in the riskier bearer form as they valued the superior liquidity — being able to sell government bonds fast to raise cold, hard dollars. Subsequently, by the early 1960s, more than 90% of marketable Treasury securities (bonds tradeable on the secondary market) were issued in bearer form. Demand for registered Treasury bills was so scarce that the Treasury never made these available for purchase.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ItyO!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc6849739-c687-4a5c-bbaa-e9457d35b150_6530x5264.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ItyO!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc6849739-c687-4a5c-bbaa-e9457d35b150_6530x5264.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

But as physical bearer bonds required preservation from loss, ruin, and theft, most holders hired commercial banks as custodians. These financial institutions, armed with superior infrastructure, managed the logistics (safekeeping, transport, and payment) involved with physical securities, such as clipping and sending off coupons to receive interest payments from the U.S. Treasury. These custodian banks also facilitated most secondary market transactions — the trading of already-issued Treasury securities, which involved one custodian transferring a customer’s bearer bond to its new owner at another custodian bank. The seller’s bank removed the bonds from the seller’s account in its physical vault, verified the amount, and prepped the bonds for delivery. A messenger, an actual employee of the bank, then transported the securities to the buyer’s custodian bank, obtaining a receipt of payment. Upon receiving the securities, the buyer’s custodian bank counted the bonds, verified the payment, and added the securities to the buyer’s account, storing the physical certificates in its vault.

This process usually took hours, if not days, depending on the location, growing particularly expensive for transactions spanning across cities. Trades were only cost-effective when both the buyer and seller lived near one of the [twelve Federal Reserve district banks](https://www.federalreservehistory.org/essays/federal-reserve-banks#:~:text=The%20Reserve%20Banks%20are%20decentralized,%2C%20Dallas%2C%20and%20San%20Francisco.). For a flat five-dollar fee, the Reserve Banks offered CPD (Commissioner of Public Debt) transfers, a function they performed as fiscal agents of the U.S. government.

CPD transfers were the first type of transaction that eliminated the geographical distance between U.S. Treasury buyers and sellers. The custodian bank sent the securities to its local Federal Reserve bank, which destroyed securities received from the seller while instructing the Reserve Bank located in the buyer’s city to create new bonds of equal value. This did not reduce the costs of safekeeping, lower the intra-city costs of transferring securities, or eliminate custodian banks’ need to insure themselves against loss. Still, CPD transfers laid the foundations for the modernized Treasury market: the [book-entry](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/bookentrysecurities.asp) system, an electronic method of tracking ownership without requiring physical certificates.

[

![The National Debt.US Basics of a Bond](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!6iKo!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0d4e4c04-57c1-4a8a-89cf-0db0f8a1ce5b_1200x836.jpeg "The National Debt.US Basics of a Bond")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!6iKo!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0d4e4c04-57c1-4a8a-89cf-0db0f8a1ce5b_1200x836.jpeg)

a physical Treasury security, circa 1969 - Source: Wikipedia

The transition to a modernized, paperless Treasury market began at the end of 1964, when the Fed set out to reduce the number of CPD transfers via a process called netting. Instead of processing securities transfers throughout the day, the Federal Reserve banks would compute the net amount of transaction requests from each member bank for each Treasury issue at 3 pm and send information to banks to settle transactions via physical delivery. This approach, which operated as an intraday book-entry system, became known as the GSCA (Government Securities Clearing Arrangement). The GSCA, though, was not a fully electronic system. The Fed’s innovation failed to reduce or eliminate many of the problems with physical Treasury securities. Despite lowering costs, the GSCA did not free up vault space or eliminate [coupon clipping](https://www.dictionary.com/browse/coupon-clipper). But more importantly, Treasury securities still required physical delivery.

Nevertheless, the infrastructure that emerged from the GSCA enticed Fed officials to investigate paperless systems further. Yet the evolution of the Treasury market was spurred by more than a simple drive to improve efficiency. Instead, as usual, a crisis compelled monetary leaders to make substantial changes. A few years after the GSCA had passed its trial stage, the number of reported securities thefts had skyrocketed, prompting the Fed to accelerate the development of a nationwide book-entry system. The first limited version went live on January 1st, 1968, allowing member banks, but not their customers, to convert both bearer and registered physical securities into book-entry form, eliminating vault space and coupon clipping for certain Treasury holdings.

Eighteen months later, the Fed expanded the system to support securities deposited in Federal Reserve banks for any purpose, including securities used in its [open market operations](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/openmarket.htm). The most significant change in this expansion was that securities pledged by member banks to third parties were converted to book entry. To facilitate this, Federal Reserve banks started taking on a more prominent role as custodians as well as fiscal agents. Subsequently, all bearer and registered securities deposited in Federal Reserve banks were easily converted to book-entry form.

[

![The Federal Reserve Leased Wire System in 1953, from the Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond 1953 Annual Report](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!7ofj!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fff374f28-b0e6-4bbd-acba-2c30855939ce_720x450.jpeg "The Federal Reserve Leased Wire System in 1953, from the Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond 1953 Annual Report")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!7ofj!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fff374f28-b0e6-4bbd-acba-2c30855939ce_720x450.jpeg)

The 1953 Fedwire system visualized — Source: The Richmond Fed

By 1970, the Fed had grown increasingly mindful of the advantages of a paperless Treasury market. At the time, its private wire system, Fedwire, had also become fully electronic. From what began as a telegraphic communication system in the 1920s, the Fed’s wire system was upgraded multiple times to meet an increasing volume of messages. A telegraph system was replaced with a teletype system, which was surmounted by a paper-type system by the 1950s. But ultimately, computer technology superseded all other formats. In 1971, the Fed introduced a fully computerized version called Sigma-5, enabling the central bank to maintain the cash and securities accounts of all twelve Federal Reserve banks and its members electronically.

By March 1973, the last two remaining types of securities had been added to the nationwide book-entry system: securities owned by member banks classed as “trading assets” and securities stored in vaults on behalf of their customers. The Fed was about to achieve its ultimate objective: a fully electronic market for U.S. sovereign debt. The issuance of physical certificates representing government obligations could be eliminated, and replaced by the creation of electronic records. Each major market participant throughout America could now conduct Treasury market transactions through electronic means.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!N_DT!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff8d486e4-fc0a-4cfc-b80f-cc5b5cca3420_6152x4071.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!N_DT!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff8d486e4-fc0a-4cfc-b80f-cc5b5cca3420_6152x4071.png)

Upon the completion of the book-entry system in 1973, the share of physical Treasury securities began to decline rapidly, so much so that U.S. Treasury officials considered slowing the issuance of physical securities. By 1977, around the time the book-entry system had become an efficient machine, the last physical bearer bill was issued, followed five years later by bearer notes and bonds. Issuance of registered bonds, meanwhile, ceased in 1986.

In that same year, the Treasury introduced a new system, TreasuryDirect, part of which remains in use today. This electronic system enabled anyone, including retail investors, to purchase Treasuries in book-entry form via phone and mail. By the 2000s, following a decade or so of technological innovation, the U.S. Treasury updated the system by accepting online purchases of government securities. The Treasury still offered book-entry sales via mail and telephone orders but characterized this as the Legacy TreasuryDirect service in 2014. Yet another decade of rapid technological advancement had compelled the U.S. Treasury to discourage the use of the legacy platform in favor of its modernized online version.

Fast forward to today, after sixty years of experimentation and innovation, the U.S. Treasury market’s plumbing looks indistinguishable from its physical predecessor. Moreover, the repo and futures markets have emerged, binding with the secondary market to fuel a well-oiled sovereign debt machine. The complexity and obscurity of the Treasury market have consequently increased to a point where its inner workings have become challenging to decipher. But, as we’re about to find out, it’s not an impossible task.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!lAJO!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff757a8ff-81ba-46cc-a7cb-b40b33a00f89_5366x4246.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!lAJO!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff757a8ff-81ba-46cc-a7cb-b40b33a00f89_5366x4246.png)

the U.S. Treasury market, as of today

The U.S. government currently operates three modernized book-entry systems for Treasury securities: the commercial book-entry system (CBES), TreasuryDirect, and Legacy TreasuryDirect. Securities remain in one of these systems until maturity or work their way between systems via intermediaries, such as banks and broker-dealers. Minus transferring securities onto Legacy TreasuryDirect, records can be transferred to and from each book-entry system. Customers of TreasuryDirect, for instance, can wire securities to CBES accounts associated with their broker, and vice versa. In every scenario, the book entries in each system are updated by the U.S. Treasury via [Fedwire](https://www.frbservices.org/financial-services/securities).

After its auction, each security begins its life either on the TreasuryDirect platform or CBES. The U.S. Treasury creates a new book-entry security on the appropriate system while receiving payment in its bank account at the Fed: the Treasury General Account ([TGA](https://www.fiscal.treasury.gov/tga/)). When retail customers purchase Treasuries (i.e. through TreasuryDirect), the security and its associated records are created and stored on the TreasuryDirect book-entry system, maintained by the Federal Reserve. Retail customers ultimately pay for securities via bank deposits, prompting their commercial bank to wire reserves, using the Fed’s [National Settlement Service](https://www.frbservices.org/resources/financial-services/wires/operating-hours.html) (NSS) or other [Fedwire](https://www.frbservices.org/financial-services/wires) services, to the TGA.

Institutional customers, meanwhile, can bid for Treasuries directly in auctions without going through an intermediary using [TAAPS](https://treasurydirect.gov/auctions/taaps/), the Treasury Automated Auction Processing System. After a sale, on the date of issuance, the investor’s selected depository institution (or if the depository institution is the investor, the depository institution itself) receives the security in its book-entry account on CBES and pays using reserves in its Fed master (Fed Funds) account. Through bespoke systems and interfaces, the depository institution then displays to the investor its newly purchased Treasury.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8CcL!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd5aef96a-7a3d-4dba-87a4-4410d2bc2e75_5984x6140.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!8CcL!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd5aef96a-7a3d-4dba-87a4-4410d2bc2e75_5984x6140.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

The most common transaction in the primary market involves the Federal Reserve’s [primary dealers](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb50c7722-5579-4572-8ea1-502a2dfcd56c_5488x4898.png) buying most of the auctioned securities to distribute among a wide range of clients, from asset managers to hedge funds. Compared to direct trading with the U.S. Treasury, primary dealer activity generates intricate flows, intertwining many of the complex systems that have emerged over the past few decades.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cDjn!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d226749-8edd-4608-adb4-6753cc4f357a_5879x6257.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cDjn!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d226749-8edd-4608-adb4-6753cc4f357a_5879x6257.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

First, at approximately 9:00 am on the issuance date, the U.S. Treasury issues securities by creating book entries on CBES and adding these to the securities accounts of the depository institution acting as a custodian for the primary dealer. Unlike TreasuryDirect’s book-entry system, CBES consists of multiple tiers. At the top level lies the Federal Reserve Banks, which maintain book-entry accounts via [Fedwire](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/f/fedwire.asp) for depository institutions (mostly banks), foreign central banks, government-sponsored enterprises, and the U.S. Treasury. Depository institutions use their top-level credentials to maintain securities accounts for clients without top-level access, mostly primary dealers and other broker-dealers. These “lower tier” entities then manage a separate set of books for their clients, such as hedge funds and individual investors, by referencing the book-entry records stored at the top level of CBES. Subsequently, not even the Federal Reserve or the U.S. Treasury know the identities of security holders. Only lower-tier entities retain such information. All the U.S. government witnesses are book entries changing hands in the accounts of top-level entities that maintain ownership records for customers at lower tiers.

Meanwhile, at the time of issuance, primary dealers pay for Treasuries using cash in their clearing accounts at the Bank of New York Mellon (BNYM), the sole clearing bank for every primary dealer. Since primary dealers aren’t depository institutions like banks, they don’t have access to a Fed master account, which enables direct payments to the U.S. government. Instead, the BNYM settles payments on behalf of the primary dealers by debiting their clearing accounts and wiring bank reserves to the Fed. The central bank then removes these reserves and credits the Treasury’s bank account, the TGA, by the same amount. The Fedwire system, through a series of network messages, automates the entire process.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Rdto!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F49769a7c-d5f1-4057-9116-20fd550f7e61_5420x2571.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Rdto!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F49769a7c-d5f1-4057-9116-20fd550f7e61_5420x2571.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

After acquiring Treasuries in the primary market at auction, primary dealers sell their inventory to customers in the secondary market, also known as the “Treasury cash market” or simply the “cash market”. When a dealer sells Treasury securities to customers — or other dealers, both parties confirm trade details and send instructions to the BNYM, which acts as the sole settlement agent for U.S. Treasuries. The [$44 trillion custodial giant](https://www.bnymellon.com/content/dam/bnymellon/documents/pdf/investor-relations/annual-report-2022.pdf) handles most of the clearing of securities, cash, and bank reserves necessary to settle secondary market trades.

The cash market is divided into two distinct parts: the interdealer segment, where dealers source liquidity and hedge customer trades among themselves, and the dealer-to-customer segment, where dealers provide liquidity from the interdealer market and their inventories to customers. The interdealer space, which makes up over half of the cash market’s trading volume, has seen the rise of a relatively novel type of participant: PTFs (principal trading firms), high-frequency trading outlets seeking to profit from mispricings while providing liquidity. PTFs, buy-side firms like hedge funds, and dealers combine to fuel over $750 billion in daily secondary market volume via central limit order books (CLOBs) — similar to [equities](https://www.wealthspire.com/financial-dictionary/equities/#:~:text=Equities%20are%20shares%20issued%20by,on%20the%20fund's%20investment%20objective.) trading platforms. The dealer-to-customer segment, meanwhile, mostly uses request-for-quote (RFQ) platforms, where dealers and PTFs display quotes to customers. The secondary market also offers bespoke venues, such as “match auctions”, which promote efficient trading of old, illiquid notes and bonds. The sophistication of the secondary market remains unrivaled.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ohCI!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6a218aa-7d9f-48fe-917d-6cad5ca5a8c6_6514x6026.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ohCI!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd6a218aa-7d9f-48fe-917d-6cad5ca5a8c6_6514x6026.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Yet, every time the Treasury market’s intricacy seems to have peaked, the U.S. empire’s debt goliath evolves once again. Over the past year, another plan to transform the Treasury market’s plumbing has arisen, with monetary leaders seeking to enforce central clearing — where a central counterparty (CCP) clears payments on behalf of both buyers and sellers — on most secondary market trades. According to a recent S.E.C. (Securities and Exchange Commission) [proposal](https://www.sec.gov/news/press-release/2022-162), transactions between a direct participant and interdealer brokers, hedge funds, levered accounts, or other broker-dealers must be centrally cleared. Implementing this step, though, will prove a significant challenge.

Presently, just 13% of cash market transactions are centrally cleared through the [DTCC](https://www.dtcc.com/), the sole CCP that provides central clearing of U.S. Treasuries. Moreover, smaller players have grown skeptical of regulators’ plans for a centrally-cleared market. Some are concerned it will increase trading costs, leading to reduced participation and thus decreased liquidity and resilience. The outcome of a faulty implementation, as with most recent actions by monetary authorities, will likely be an even greater [decline](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2023/10/how-has-treasury-market-liquidity-evolved-in-2023/) in liquidity and depth, the opposite of what the Treasury market’s plumbing requires. To preserve the functioning of the most systemically important market globally, central planners must facilitate a smooth structural evolution, just as the margin for error falls to its lowest level.

_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Treasury Market Evolution - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> U.S. officials have triggered a major shift in Treasury market structure

---
_— Update: The SEC [extended](https://www.sec.gov/newsroom/press-releases/2025-43) the clearing deadlines mentioned by up to one year._

After years of deliberation following multiple crises, America’s monetary leaders have unveiled their plan to fortify the U.S. Treasury market. The inner workings of the most systemically important debt market globally, having already undergone significant changes, will once again be transformed. The latest Treasury Market Evolution™ is about to commence.

In the mid-2000s, the complexity of the secondary Treasury market was almost nonexistent. Major dealer banks and broker-dealers dominated volumes in the interdealer segment, where they traded among themselves, while distributing most of the liquidity to clients in the dealer-to-customer region. Just as the market appeared to have peaked in sophistication, however, a new type of player emerged from the shadows: principal trading firms ([PTFs](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/principal-trading-firm-activity-in-treasury-cash-markets-20200804.html)), high-frequency traders seeking to profit through arbitrage while providing liquidity to dealers. Upon developing a range of high-frequency trading algorithms, PTFs began to conquer interdealer markets — the largest segment by volume, taking market share from even the most prominent players. A new age of complexity in the Treasury cash market was thus destined to emerge.

Before PTFs began to take over in the late 2000s, the interdealer market consisted largely of primary dealers and other dealers, the largest of which were all members of the [FICC](https://www.dtcc.com/about/businesses-and-subsidiaries/ficc) (Fixed Income Clearing Corporation), the sole central clearing provider (CCP) for U.S. Treasuries. The majority of secondary market transactions involved trading via the electronic platforms of interdealer brokers (IDBs), the major intermediaries of the interdealer market. As both IDBs and primary dealers held memberships with the FICC, most secondary market trades were centrally cleared. Though that was about to change. While gaining significant market share, PTFs did not meet the strict requirements needed to become FICC members, failing to obtain direct access to central clearing. Subsequently, the first “centrally cleared era” of the Treasury cash market was coming to an end.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!iNuf!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F24a41ad8-b9c2-485b-abff-ff9e1d6f07fe_5366x4246.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!iNuf!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F24a41ad8-b9c2-485b-abff-ff9e1d6f07fe_5366x4246.png)

the Treasury cash market, highlighted in green

The rise in the number of FICC non-members (PTFs, smaller dealers, and buy-side firms like hedge funds) compared to FICC members (primary dealers, larger dealers, banks, and IDBs) caused a surge in “bilateral” trading. Instead of centrally clearing through the FICC, non-member trades were cleared and settled through various separate entities, from clearing agents to the Federal Reserve and its [Fedwire](https://www.frbservices.org/financial-services/securities) service. Interdealer brokers still intermediated the vast majority of trading, though, increasing the market’s complexity. The rise of “hybrid trading” emerged, in which the first leg between an FICC member (say, a dealer) and an IDB (interdealer broker) was centrally cleared while the last leg between an IDB and the FICC member’s customer (say, a hedge fund) was cleared bilaterally. FICC non-members could still access central clearing through a dealer’s [prime brokerage](https://www.investopedia.com/articles/professionals/110415/role-prime-broker.asp#:~:text=Prime%20brokerage%20refers%20to%20a,%2C%20securities%20lending%2C%20and%20more.) service, yet the increased cost drove firms to trade without central clearing. These factors combined to produce a sharp decline in the share of centrally cleared trades, slowly reducing the market’s exposure to FICC protections, and thus eroding Treasury market resiliency.

Fast forward to today and countless market hiccups later, around 70 to 80% of secondary market transactions are now bilaterally cleared. According to a recent [G-30](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/g/g-30.asp) [report](https://scholar.harvard.edu/sites/scholar.harvard.edu/files/stein/files/g30_paper.pdf), only “20% of commitments to settle U.S. Treasury security trades” are centrally cleared through the FICC’s platform. Meanwhile, in the interdealer segment, the largest PTFs have gained equal market share to the largest broker-dealers, accounting for half the volume on interdealer broker platforms and ~55% of the volume on “on-the-run” (the most recently issued) 10-year Treasuries.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oVRI!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6de1f054-3bf9-4368-8633-def3ada7739b_6559x5038.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oVRI!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6de1f054-3bf9-4368-8633-def3ada7739b_6559x5038.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Monetary officials, however, have responded. Vowing to end the longstanding “uncleared era” in America’s sovereign debt market, the SEC (Securities and Exchange Commission) has [announced](https://www.sec.gov/news/press-release/2023-247) that more secondary market trades must be centrally cleared by December 31st, 2025. Market players must now adjust to comply with a new centrally cleared regime, prompting not only an extensive rewiring of the Treasury market’s plumbing but a large transfer of risk to the FICC, the lone central clearing provider. What does that mean in practice? Let’s go deeper into the mechanics.

With centrally cleared trades, a central counterparty (CCP) guarantees the settlement of cash and securities via a process called novation. The FICC, acting as the Treasury market’s CCP, “novates” trades by signing contracts with both buyer and seller to absorb losses resulting from a counterparty default. While uncleared (i.e. bilateral) trades expose the buyer and the seller to each other’s credit risk between trade booking and settlement, central clearing prevents settlement failures when either entity defaults on its obligations. In return, participants must follow the FICC’s strict rulebook, contribute capital to a “default fund”, pay clearing fees, and post (cash) margin to secure trades numerous times a day.

In a typical transaction, an FICC member buys a Treasury from another FICC member via an IDB (interdealer broker). The FICC novates both legs, one between the buying FICC member and the IDB and one between the IDB and the selling FICC member. The FICC’s platform handles all the logistics of each leg, from netting (i.e. removing redundant trades) to risk management to clearing and settlement. As the new regulatory deadline approaches, centrally clearing trades like these will become the norm.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!vHUF!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0a83c2d0-1e1f-4c95-9ea7-658a5ee33b1f_6632x5758.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!vHUF!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0a83c2d0-1e1f-4c95-9ea7-658a5ee33b1f_6632x5758.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

In fact, it’s clear now that the regulatory endgame involves centrally clearing almost all secondary market transactions, inserting the FICC between entities forming the financial chains of the most influential market globally. The latest reforms are merely the first phase. These actions will increase fees and capital requirements, plus require significant investment to onboard a wider number of market participants onto the FICC’s platform. Still, these are the consequences that authorities have been willing to bear. In response to a [backlash](https://www.ropesgray.com/en/insights/alerts/2023/12/sec-adopts-rules-requiring-clearing-of-us-treasury-transactions), they have somewhat offset the impact by [relaxing](https://www.ropesgray.com/en/insights/alerts/2023/12/sec-adopts-rules-requiring-clearing-of-us-treasury-transactions) margin requirements for FICC members when facilitating customer trades, making it easier for all parties to centrally clear indirectly through the FICC’s platform.

In what they’ve [called](https://www.sec.gov/files/rules/final/2023/34-99149.pdf) an “incremental first step”, monetary leaders have mandated central clearing for entities that likely risk the stability of the Treasury market and the FICC. This includes speculative trades between dealers and their customers in the dealer-to-customer repo market, such as primary dealers providing liquidity to hedge funds (and other leveraged accounts) participating in highly leveraged “[basis trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-unwind)”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BMXP!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F460712b9-43f6-4c60-aae6-4e938d4a656d_6315x5225.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BMXP!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F460712b9-43f6-4c60-aae6-4e938d4a656d_6315x5225.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Low risk-takers, such as unlevered players who buy Treasuries as investments, are some of the few players that won’t require central clearing — at least, not in this phase. Meanwhile, in the interdealer market, any entity that brings buyers and sellers together on trading platforms must use central clearing, implying that IDBs must clear most trades through the FICC. This will encourage practically every leg of an interdealer trade to be centrally cleared, helping to prevent a large buildup of counterparty risk.

Risk, of course, can only be transferred, not eliminated. Nevertheless, regulators are betting on central clearing to improve market liquidity and stability, knowing the FICC will likely grow into more of a [single point of failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single_point_of_failure). The central clearing giant, however, maintains multiple layers of protection before it can fail, such as maintaining sufficient buffers to survive two of its largest members defaulting simultaneously.

But if it does come close to collapse, the FICC will have grown into the key wheel greaser of the Treasury market. Already designated as systemically important, the FICC holds accounts with the Federal Reserve, and upon a majority vote from the Board plus approval from the Treasury Secretary, the FICC can gain access to emergency liquidity. With no other CCPs able to provide central clearing, and the FICC becoming the major glue holding the Treasury market plumbing together, monetary leaders will likely keep the central clearing giant alive, at whatever expense.

_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Great Flattening - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> the global regulatory complex has declared war on rate spikes

---
A longstanding loophole exploited by banks globally is set to evaporate. By preventing the most notorious instances of “window-dressing,” a frowned-upon practice designed to skirt regulatory constraints, the global regulatory complex will soon deter banks from evading rules imposed on them following the Great Financial Crisis (GFC). Subsequently, the signature “rate spikes” emerging in repo markets on key financial dates may not only become less extreme but more infrequent. Monetary leaders’ attempt at a “great flattening” has been initiated.

At the end of December 2023, the Federal Reserve’s RRP (reverse repo) facility — the U.S. central bank’s “shock absorber” for excess dollars unable to be invested elsewhere — continued to drain. Plunging toward zero, the Fed’s facility had shed trillions in surplus cash now fleeing to higher-yielding assets such as [t-bills](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/t/treasurybill.asp), flows that would soon alter the [dynamics](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-reverse-repo-endgame) in repo markets. The enormous overload of money that had poured into repo markets via the Fed’s COVID-19 liquidity injections was about to dry up, prompting market participants to begin to trade primarily among themselves. Instead of rates remaining pinned to the overnight rate the Fed paid on its RRPs — dubbed O/N[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-1-146276672) RRP, private sector entities would start to determine where most repo rates printed. The total “unzombification” of repo markets was fast approaching.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zm1i!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6dbb2bcc-de45-465e-b8ed-f8f65a5b4c16_6157x4524.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zm1i!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6dbb2bcc-de45-465e-b8ed-f8f65a5b4c16_6157x4524.png)

excess cash (plus balance sheet relief expiring for banks) has caused repo rates to fall and linger near O/N RRP: the Fed’s repo rates “floor”

Suddenly, however, on December 29th, 2023, the multi-trillion-dollar descent in the level of Fed RRPs was halted temporarily by a ~$200 billion rise in volume. [Repo rates](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) simultaneously began to skyrocket, with the cost to borrow cash in the interdealer ([GCF](https://conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part)) and dealer-to-customer ([DVP](https://www.concoda.com/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part-93d)) markets — ultimately financing the [elaborate trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-relative-value-trading?utm_source=publication-search) of hedge funds and others — rising sharply. The cause? Yet another “quarter-end”: the last day of each quarter on the financial calendar and the most likely to encounter volatility. As with most quarter-end (but especially year-end[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-2-146276672)) dates, the prominent repo dealers were pulling back severely from market-making in the most crucial dollar funding ecosystem. The trading desks of the major broker-dealers and dealer subsidiaries of the largest banks ([BHCs](https://www.fedpartnership.gov/bank-life-cycle/grow-shareholder-value/bank-holding-companies#:~:text=Most%20banks%20in%20the%20U.S.,state%20nonmember%2C%20or%20national%20bank.)) — all operating as [primary dealers](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb50c7722-5579-4572-8ea1-502a2dfcd56c_5488x4898.png) — delayed their duties to unwind or rearrange trades linked to the most infamous regulatory exploit in the modern era.

For a decade or so, via one method or another, the banking giants have bypassed numerous constraints proposed by the [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm) Accords, a sea of regulations introduced after the GFC to limit excessive bank risk-taking “once and for all.” In what’s known as “window-dressing,” some dealers (primarily foreign dealers) have yet to be required to declare their regulatory metrics using daily averages, the absence of which has allowed them to expand their balance sheets over lengthy periods in which they don’t need to reveal costly or uncompliant exposures to regulators. Before the deadline to disclose their activities, banks have quickly unwound their soon-to-be illicit trades.

Rather than serving as fully active market makers on “period-end” dates, such as [quarter-ends](https://www.vidyard.com/sales-glossary/end-of-quarter/#:~:text=The%20%E2%80%9Cend%20of%20a%20quarter,period%20on%20a%20financial%20calendar.) and year-ends, dealers have been rewiring their balance sheets to appear as if they took much less risk during certain timeframes than in practice. Consequently, on and around crucial reporting periods, banks’ window-dressing has created temporary “air pockets” of illiquidity (even [turmoil](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame)) in repo markets. Because most repos[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-3-146276672) — short-term cash loans secured against collateral — can be rolled over [forever](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part-93d) but unwound swiftly the next day, these are the first assets to be dumped by banks whenever they hold excess exposures and reporting dates grow near. Liquidity in repo markets thus grows scarce. Rates must adjust higher to attract those willing to make markets with limited balance sheets, ultimately provoking infamous rate spikes.

At the end of December 2023, the largest repo liquidity providers were either reducing or reshuffling their balance sheets to report more favorable metrics, inducing one of the most bizarre yet typical scenarios in money markets. With regulatory limits now compulsory, banks with available but inflexible balance sheets stepped in by lending cash to dealers and other market participants at hefty spreads. Rates even surpassed what the Fed offered via its Standing Repo Facility (SRF), a mechanism that supposedly enforced an upper limit on repo rates. Since the SRF was set to open (as usual) at 1:30pm — long after the peak repo trading hours of 8-10am, dealers were forced to secure dollars via private markets at rates above the Fed’s repo “ceiling.” Meanwhile, with their usual dealer counterparties unwilling to provide liquidity, money market funds (MMFs) — the major cash investors in repo markets — were forced to accept a lower return on their cash by parking it at the Fed’s [RRP facility](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-triparty-repo-market).

Although somewhat defective, the Fed’s tools for enforcing upper and lower limits in its [repo hierarchy](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-mechanics-of-the) were able to stem a full-blown liquidity squeeze in both directions — the RRP providing a (soft) rates floor and the SRF providing a (soft) rates ceiling.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!_Hcu!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3e71d75a-3760-4111-aa9a-730ee2843df9_1796x1370.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!_Hcu!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3e71d75a-3760-4111-aa9a-730ee2843df9_1796x1370.png)

the intricacies of period-end repo rate spikes | repo rates [primer](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-money-markets)

Fast forward to today, and repo market participants have witnessed countless further rate spikes on key financial dates. Only last month, [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr) (the Secured Overnight Financing Rate), the Fed’s primary measure of repo rates, rose again to recent heights, [causing](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-07-02/clogged-bank-balance-sheets-cause-key-repo-funding-rate-to-spike) a stir in the media. With increased money market unrest, regulators appear to have witnessed enough to want to finally avert most, if not all, banks from evading Basel III and provoking market disruptions. The [global regulatory complex](https://www.bis.org/about/index.htm?m=1001), following the usual lengthy pause, has reached its near-maximum tolerance for allowing the most observable regulatory arbitrage in dollar funding markets to continue unabated. Officials have commenced a slow purge of the most significant incentives, attempting a _Great Flattening_ of repo rate volatility. Yet, this flattener may prove more difficult than they expect. Catalysts other than the Basel III Accords will continue to spur instability. Let’s dive deeper into the plumbing.

Slowly but surely, monetary leaders have “shockingly” deduced that a loophole in major post-GFC regulations has spurred the majority of window-dressing by financial behemoths. As a recent ECB document [shows](https://www.ecb.europa.eu/pub/pdf/scpwps/ecb.wp2771~fc55bab0d6.en.pdf), two constraints provide the most significant incentives for banks to game the system. The first involves the Basel III [leverage ratio](https://www.bis.org/publ/bcbs270.htm), designed to prevent banks from expanding their balance sheets without raising adequate capital to absorb potential losses. Banks initially were required to report a “snapshot” of their leverage ratios at quarter-ends, meaning they could lever up their balance sheets beyond acceptable limits during a quarter and then quickly de-lever to report a compliant ratio. The Fed swiftly stamped out this arbitrage by forcing banks to calculate their snapshot using daily averages over the quarter — instead of whatever their leverage ratio equaled on the reporting date. But as each country implemented its own version of Basel III, this modification only applied to U.S. banks. Foreign banks, especially European banks, have been sidestepping their country’s implementation of the leverage ratio for years, window-dressing freely without much backlash.

Alongside the leverage ratio, the other major motivation to window-dress involves G-SIBs (global systemically important banks) trying to lower their “G-SIB surcharges”: an extra amount of capital they must hold to account for their global risk footprint. The Basel III methodology separates banks that qualify as G-SIBs into five buckets — “Bucket 4” being the most systemically important and “Bucket 0” being the least — based on bank size, complexity, interconnectedness, flexibility, and cross-border activity. The BIS (Bank of International Settlements), the architect of Basel III, publishes an overview of which banking goliaths pose the largest threat to global financial stability (if they were to fail) via a “[G-SIB dashboard](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/gsib/index.htm)”. The higher the bucket, the harsher the surcharge and the more capital that a G-SIB[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-4-146276672) must maintain.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!e074!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3f1c7f99-9e66-44c2-a8e6-60fca8a6f0c7_1573x1427.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!e074!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3f1c7f99-9e66-44c2-a8e6-60fca8a6f0c7_1573x1427.png)

the BIS’s G-SIB (global systemically important bank) dashboard | BIS

Like with Basel III’s leverage ratio, each nation implements a custom version of the G-SIB framework. The Fed, for instance, mandates JPMorgan, the only bank in the highest risk bucket, to hold an extra 4.5% capital (_in grey below_), 1% higher than Basel III guidelines.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KVZZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F39473788-fa82-416b-aa60-41e69198c474_1900x1085.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KVZZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F39473788-fa82-416b-aa60-41e69198c474_1900x1085.png)

the Fed’s G-SIB surcharge increasing JPM’s capital ratio by 4.5% | JPMorgan

Yet despite its sophistication, the G-SIB surcharge possesses a major flaw: banks only need to disclose their scores via snapshots at the end of the year. Just before year-end, the financial giants sharply increase their window-dressing activity to decrease (or avoid) surcharges, potentially spurring market upheavals. The BIS [reported](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/publ/wp42.pdf) that the G-SIB framework has been responsible for “around half” of year-end contractions in banks’ derivatives exposures, such as repos. Regardless, even though this is likely exacerbating most year-end rate spikes, each nation has yet to force G-SIBs to calculate their scores using daily averages, enabling the largest banks to window-dress and lower their required dose of capital.

As reporting dates for both these major window-dressing catalysts grow near, dealers use their repo trading books as sacrificial lambs. Repo desks de-lever either by allowing existing repos to unwind or, more likely, “rewire” existing repos into “nettable” trades, which don’t increase the size of their balance sheet or count as leverage. The repo liabilities and reverse repo assets of dealers, known as matched repos, become nettable or “net” when executed with the same counterparty and expire on the same date, offsetting a variety of financial hazards — and hence nettable under global accounting standards. Netting has thus evolved into the key mechanism for dealers running matched repo books[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-5-146276672) to [survive](https://www.concoda.com/p/infographic-how-dealers-survive-under) under Basel III’s brutal leverage restrictions.

Shuffling repos around to window-dress your balance sheet, though, remains one of the most challenging tasks for traders on large dealer desks. The repo market is structured so that dealers fund most of their operations in the [triparty market](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market), the “dealer funding market”, where MMFs serve as the only significant cash lender. As MMFs only lend and never borrow, they can’t be counterparties to both sides of a dealer’s matched book, prompting a large portion of a dealer's balance sheet to become “un-nettable” during a quarter.

Dealers could net some trades with MMFs via central clearing, as a clearinghouse (CCP) becomes the counterparty to all repo lenders and borrowers. The triparty market, however, has failed to adopt it. Subsequently, for dealers to window-dress their balance sheets via netting, they must replace their triparty funding with centrally cleared repos. Dealers turn to the interdealer markets — namely, the [GCF](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part) repo and [DVP](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part-93d) repo services operated by the Fixed Income Clearing Corporation (FICC), the only markets that offer centrally cleared repo trading. The FICC, as the sole CCP in repo, becomes the lender to every borrower and the borrower to every lender, supercharging the pool of potentially nettable repos. The other critical dynamic is that interdealer markets are effectively interbank markets. Each major bank operates both a vast dealer and commercial banking business, which reside in separate legal entities within the same bank holding company. A bank’s dealer and commercial bank entity both have access to interdealer repos, but each one serves a different purpose. Dealers make the majority of markets, while banks take over and provide funding (via spare reserves) when nobody else can or will.

Nevertheless, as one of _Conks’_ proprietary graphics above demonstrates, a dealer’s switch from un-nettable to nettable repos on period-end dates is far from smooth sailing. Fellow dealers are already pulling back to window-dress, and commercial banks won’t lend in repo until rates shoot above [IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB) (interest on reserve balances), the rate the Fed pays commercial banks on their reserves. What’s more, it’s reporting time, and the strictest of regulations grow binding. Spreads must now widen enough for banks with surplus reserves, the last private source of cash used to settle market imbalances, to “step in” and earn a worthwhile return for providing crucial liquidity. Rates to borrow cash could climb as much as 50bps (0.50%) above IORB — a greater cost than borrowing from the Fed’s standing repo facility.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!B13M!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5f412ec6-811f-41d4-bc3a-fa040c776b54_5574x3768.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!B13M!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5f412ec6-811f-41d4-bc3a-fa040c776b54_5574x3768.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

If banks haven’t stepped in by early afternoon, the U.S. central bank’s unlimited balance sheet comes to the rescue. At 1:30 pm New York time, the SRF provides Fed cash via repos to primary dealers and other pre-approved [counterparties](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/standing-repo-facility-counterparties), primarily depository institutions, which pass the liquidity on to other repo participants, such as hedge funds. Just before, between 12:45 pm and 1:15 pm, MMFs who can’t find dealers to lend to during tight liquidity conditions must surrender and lend cash to the Fed at the O/N RRP rate, the lowest return available. By the repo “market close” at around 5 pm, all market imbalances have been neutralized by banks or the Fed, and the window-dressing fiasco pauses until the next period-end approaches.

But now, officials have begun stamping out window-dressing incentives, attempting a _Great Flattening_ of money market volatility. Regulators worldwide, albeit slowly, have been disincentivizing the largest foreign banks and broker-dealers, which presently dominate repo volumes, from bypassing Basel III. These financial behemoths will have to adapt, no longer leveraging up intra-quarter and unwinding at period-end dates to bypass restrictions. In Europe, since last December, the European Central Bank (ECB) has increased leverage ratios, while EU regulators have required banks to use quarterly averages, not snapshots, when reporting. Other central banks will likely follow suit. The regulatory crackdown is now in motion.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3VE_!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2467d0fd-85f1-4d60-b51d-abfe22d52d40_5904x4348.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3VE_!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2467d0fd-85f1-4d60-b51d-abfe22d52d40_5904x4348.png)

interdealer rates have started spiking on month-ends

Alas, for regulators, an inconvenient truth persists: repo rate instability has prevailed, and now during periods unconnected to Basel III. Other catalysts that prompt window-dressing and subsequent money market spikes still exist. For instance, even the largest, most systemically important U.S. banks could [de-lever](https://research.bangor.ac.uk/portal/files/47861251/Euro_Fin_Management_2022_Jaafar_Persistency_of_window_dressing_practices_in_the_U_S_repo_markets_after_the_GFC_The.pdf) their repo exposures to reduce fees paid to the FDIC for deposit insurance. One high-level source also revealed that dealers have begun window-dressing, not only because of Basel III but to appear risk-averse to their firm’s internal risk divisions at the end of each month — perhaps somewhat explaining the emergence of month-end spikes _(see above)_ in repo rates.

Despite recent central bank crackdowns, money market rates are set to remain volatile. Regulators’ attempts at a _Great Flattening_ will eliminate some incentives to window-dress, but the ability of private actors to game the system and prompt volatility in other ways should never be underestimated. An uptick in usage of the Fed’s standing repo facility is very much still on the table.
